THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Tuesday, 22 September 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:

Community Youth Support Scheme
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament Assembled. This petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that by terminating the Community Youth Support Scheme the unemployed young will be severely disadvantaged. The Com-, munity Youth Support Scheme operates as an essential service in the Acquisition of employment skills, and therefore plays an essential role in the Commonwealth Governments Manpower Training Schemes, It is also the principal mode of involvement for the community in the significant problems of youth unemployment.

O~mmunity Youth Support Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled.
The humbie petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth their anger and condemnation at the Fraser government's decision to abolish all Community Youth Support Schemes on the 31st October 1981.
Further they denounce the decision for the disastrous effects it will have on growing numbers of unemployed young people around Australia.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government immediately and fully reverse its decision and restore complete funding to the Community Youth Support Scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Sinclair, Mrs Kelly and Mr O'Keefe.
Petitions received.

Community Youth Support Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Victoria respectfully showeth:
that the serious youth unemployment problem is a direct result of the community's failure to create sufficient jobs.
In these circumstances the decision to abolish Community Youth Support Schemes (CYSS) is a socially retrogressive step. The CYSS Projects provide valuable support services and work training for many young unemployed.
We the undersigned are concerned that the closure of CYSS Projects may lead young unemployed to become involved in anti-social behaviour such as crime, vandalism, prostitution, rebellious behaviour and mob action. There is the even more frightening prospect that many more young people will give up hope of obtaining a job, become idle and even contemplate suicide.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government reverse its decision to abolish CYSS Projects and provide sufficient funds to enable the various CYSS Centres to continue their valuable work with the young unemployed in the future.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Ewen Cameron, Mr Duffy, Mr Holding, Mr Howe, Mr Barry Jones and Mr Peacock.
Petitions received.
Community Youth Support Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker, and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of the Division of Ballarat respectfully showeth:
(i) that the action of the Government in terminating the Community Youth Support Scheme will have serious effects upon the life possibilities of a significant number of young people who, through circumstances totally outside their control, are not able to find any form of paid employment,
(ii) that the action of the Government fails to understand that many unemployed youth do not fit into the retraining initiatives proposed and that part of the need within the total context of school-to-work transition is to have support structures based around the Community Youth Support Scheme model,
(iii) that the failure to provide such supports will place many young people in a position of high vulnerability to crime, violence, vandalism, drug abuse, and even suicide, and that the incidence of homelessness and family breakdown will be further aggravated.
Your petitioners humbly pray that the Government give immediate attention to the re-instatement of the Community Youth Support Scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Cunningham and Mr Mildren.

Defence Services Home Loans
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectFully showeth:
That all members of the Women's Services 1939-45 War qualify as "Australian Soldier" under the Defence Service Homes Act for loans because on voluntarily joining the Australian Forces they took the oath to resist His Majesty's enemies making themselves available for combat duty in War Zones, and according to Acts and Regulations of the period the Military women had enlisted for service anywhere, some Naval women to sail in any waters, with all servicewomen available for service ill Papua New Guinea.
As those members of the Royal Australian Navy, the Australian Imperial Force and the Royal Australian Air Force of 1939-45 War who were not sent to War Zones are granted loans, refusal to grant eligibility to all Ex-servicewomen of 1939-45 War is unjust and unfair.
Your petitioners therefore pray that you amend the Defence Service Homes Act by repealing Sub-section 4 (2A) (b) (c) (d) of the Principal Act as amended by No. 137 of 1978, and grant eligibility to all members of the Women's Royal Australian Naval Service, the Australian Women's Army Service, the Women's Auxiliary Australian Air Force, the Australian Army Medical Women's Service, and the Voluntary Aid Detachment of 1939-45 War.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Barry Jones and Mr Scholes_

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in June 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that "between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent."
It is your petitioners sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of life to decide whether or not the disc.losure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility for the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position which may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrDobie.
Petition received.

Aboriginal Land Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the projected repeal by the Queensland State Government of the Aborigines Act 1971-1979 and the Torres Strait Islander Act 1971-1979 will almost certainly lead to the degazettal of all Aboriginal reserves in Queensland, as has already been the case with Aurukun and Mornington Island.
That if, as is likely, the Aboriginal communities from these reserves are placed under similar shire council legislation on fifty year leases, they will be exposed to serious encroachment on their land by mining and tourist interests.
That it is also quite clear, on past evidence, that these interests will have the full backing of the Queensland Government and the rights of Aboriginal people to land rights and self-management will not be granted by that Government.
That the Commonwealth Government has the power to legislate and acquire land for Aboriginal people under its constitutional powers since the amendments to the Constitution in 1967, but it is your petitioners' sincere belief that it failed to act in 1978 and thus, failed in its duty before the people of Aurukun and Mornington Island.

by Mr Humphreys.

Family Allowances
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the allowable amount of income to be earned by people receiving social security benefits has not been adjusted since 1972 and in the case of unemployed not since 1969.
That the $20 allowable for beneficiaries in 1972 was at a time when the benefit was $18.25 per week.
That if the same principle applied and if the amount earned had been indexed the amount now allowable would exceed $63.20 per week.
That Family Allowances have never been indexed.
That heartless, insensitive, frugal and anomalous social security benefits, allowances and rules are causing severe financial distress to many citizens and causing the breakup of families.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government will bring all allowances and benefits up to the levels which would make them equivalent in real monetary terms to the time when they were set.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrKerin.

MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS
Mr MALCOLM FRASER (Wannon-Prime Minister)-I inform the House that the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) left Australia on 18 September to attend the United Nations General Assembly in New York. The Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee) will act as Minister for Foreign Affairs until Mr Street returns on 25 September. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) left Australia on 18 September for consultations overseas. The Minister for Finance (Senator Dame Margaret Guilfoyle) will act as Treasurer until 11 October. The Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) will represent the Treasurer in this chamber.
FRASER GOVERNMENT
Notice of Motion
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition)-I give notice that, on the next day of sitting, I shall move:
That this House censures the Government for its failure to ensure proper and adequate standards of administration by senior Government Ministers, including the Ministers for Communications and Industrial Relations, and for its failure to give effect to the Prime Minister's statement of 12 March 1978 that 'Without integrity-without the appearance and reality of integrity-no government can hope to have the confidence of the people, or the trust of the people'.
Suspension of Standing Orders
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House)-I seek leave of the House to move a motion to enable the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) to move forthwith the motion of censure of which he has given notice for the next sitting day.

Mr Scholes-Mr Speaker, I take a point of order. Does the Minister recognise the motion under Standing Orders as a censure motion?
Mr SPEAKER-That point will arise after the motion has been put. Is leave granted? There being no objection, leave is granted.
Mr SINCLAIR-I move:
That so much of the Standing Orders J:>e suspended as would prevent the Leader of the Opposition moving forthwith the motion of censure of the Government of which he has given notice for the next sitting.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House)-To satisfy the curiosity of the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) I inform the House that the Government accepts the notice of motion given by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) as a motion of censure of the Government for the purposes of Standing Order 110.
Motion of Censure
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (1.54)-1 move:
That this House censures the Government for its failure to ensure proper and adequate standards of administration by senior Government Ministers, including the Ministers for Communications and Industrial Relations, and for its failure to give effect to the Prime Minister's statement of 12 March 1978 that 'Without integrity-without the appearance and reality of integrity-no government can hope to have the confidence of the people, or the trust of the people.'
The Opposition moves this motion of censure against the Government because of its persistent, discreditable record in ministerial administration. The Fraser Government reels from one scandal to the next with self-destructive determination. In six years 14 Ministers have been sacked, suspended or forced to resign. It is an unmatched record in the history of the national Parliament and it is a record which does not match the elevated principles of ministerial responsibility and integrity in government of which the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) has spoken so much in the past. Three more Ministers should go today. All will stay. The Government has decided to tough it out and to try to brazen the problem away once again. In the process, a lot of personal abuse will be heaped upon those people in this Parliament, not only on the Opposition side but also within the Government's own ranks, who have raised, quite genuinely and seriously, criticisms about defectiveness in government administration. Two will stay because they belong to the National Country Party. The other will remain because he belongs to the Prime Minister's faction in an increasingly factionalised government. He is loyal to the Prime Minister and on that rare

1574 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

characteristic, of course, is attractive. He is also very servile and fawning.
The Prime Minister' is confrohted by one National Country Party Minister who has been branded by his colleagues as a person whose word is worthless. He is confronted by two National Country Party Ministers who have displayed manifest administrative incompetence in handling the affairs of the departments for which they are responsible. He is confronted by the blundering damage of the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) and he is confronted by the public white-anting of the Government's inadequate performance by the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock). All of this is clear evidence of the disaffection and disarray within the Government's ranks and, equally worryingly, he is confronted by a Department of Primary Industry which has been exposed by a Senate standing committee as slothful, self-serving and deceitful. For all of that, for all of the searing evidence which undermines the credibility of the Government and shatters its self-confidence, the Prime Minister is prepared to provide a cast iron pledge of support for the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) and he is prepared to do it without even having read the report upon which the criticisms are based. He does not believe in evidence, he does not believe in facts; he believes in rigging the Parliament with the numbers that he has.
I quote from an article by Russell Barton in the Sydney Morning Herald last 'Saturday morning. The Prime Minister was asked whether he had confidence in Mr Sinclair, the Minister for Communications. He replied firmly:
'Obviously'.
The report also states:
Mr Fraser said yesterday he would be reading the Senate committee report over the weekend.
He had made up his mind before he had even seen the evidence. The undeniable fact is, and it is a cause of continuing aggravation within the coalition ranks, that there are special indulgences for National Country Party members of the coalition. The Prime Minister cannot shrug this scandal off as lightly as he would wish to pretend. Senator Withers was sacked over a phone call. Mr Sinclair, the Minister for Communications, is declared by his colleagues, by members of his own Party, by members of the Senate, by a Senate standing committee, unanimously, as not being a believable person. His colleagues declare that. In fact they say that in unequivocal terms at page 174 of the report from the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations

on its inquiry into the Australian Dairy Corporation and its Asian Subsidiaries. I quote:
The Committee concludes that on the balance of probabilities, Mr Sinclair's recollection of the events was not as reliable as that of Mr Webster. In other words, it appears to the Committee that on the balance of probabilities, Mr Sinclair was told of the terms of the compromise which necessarily involved a continuing breach of GATT.
The Government will, with greater vigour, seek to paint Mr Webster as a rogue. Let it go ahead and do that. What it has to accommodate in doing that is this simple declaration of the Senate committee report; that a person whom the Government will seek to declare as a rogue is more believable in the eyes of members of the Senate than the Minister for Communications.
That was not a decision that was lightly arrived at. It was a unanimous decision. Four of the six members of the committee are lawyers. Three of those four lawyers are Government members. One of those Government lawyer members is Senator Lewis, a man who is independent in his personality, with a reputation for intellectual toughness, a product of the Western Districts of Victoria and a close associate of the Prime Minister. He has not engaged on some sort of spiteful campaign to try to undermine the Minister for Communications. On the contrary, he would have weighed the evidence as fairly and as impartially as he could, according to the ethics of his profession, but with some sentiment for the Minister for Communications as a fellow member of the Government. On the basis of that evidence he found that he was forced to come down and declare that the Minister for Communications is not believable.
The Committee was clear-sighted about Mr Webster and his deficiencies. That is abundantly clear in the report which has been compiled by the Committee and which, I repeat, was endorsed unanimously. But acknowledging all of those deficiencies, the Committee declared that it preferred to believe the word of Mr Webster rather than the word of the Minister for Communications. The Minister for Communications argues that at no time until late in 1976-at no time leading up to that point-was he made aware that Australia was seriously and persistently breaching her internationai treaty obligations in relation to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade. This arose from the sale of skim milk powder. The price had fallen to the minimum level acceptable to GATT. In spite of that, a rebate of $25 a tonne was provided, which meant effectively that the GATT rules were being circumvented.
Now, the Minister displays an extraordinary curiosity in relation to this matter. A letter was

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1575

sent by Mr Webster to the Minister after he had been made aware of the circumstances-that negotiations had been conducted in South East Asia and that an amicable resolution had been arrived at in relation to the matter. Nothing further was done. No questions were raised, and no consultation was sought with the Department of Primary Industry, in spite of the fact that representatives of that Department are, by legislation, members of the, board of the Australian Dairy Corporation. They are there to represent the Government, to ensure that the Department of Primary Industry is well aware of developments taking place at that board level and to ensure, through the Department, that the Minister is well versed in relation to those developments.
In spite of all those facts, the Minister for Communications declares that he was satisfied with the letter which said nothing more than that an amicable resolution had been arrived at in terms of this problem. What sort of amicable resolution was it? A serious breach of our GATT treaty obligations had been committed-and persistently committed. That having been made clearly known to the Minister, it was a matter of great responsibility for him to ensure that those obligations were being met forthwith. He seeks to invoke the support of a Mr Wallace, a departmental representative at one stage on the ADC. He seeks to invoke him as verification of the fact that he would not have been aware of what had been taking place prior to late 1976 when he was officially advised of this breach of our treaty obligations. I will quote from page 173 of the report to establish how believable Mr Wallace is. The report states:
Regrettably Mr Wallace's evidence was so guarded and qualified and his manner so evasive that far from providing unqualified support for Mr Sinclair's version of the events, Mr Wallace at best only offered luke-warm and tentative confirmation,
That is scarcely the sort of evidence in support that one would care to call if one's reputation were dependent on the sort of contribution that person could make.
I ask honourable members to consider the baffling sequence of events which are related to these circumstances. A departmental officer, a Mr O'Bryan, sitting on the board of the Australian Dairy Corporation, at a meeting on 2 April 1976, heard discussion about what essentially were the ingredients of this breach of our international treaty obligations. Nothing was done. I draw the attention of honourable members to pages 193 and 194 of the report. I will refrain from quoting from the report as the evidence is there. Nothing

was done, he says. The Department of Primary Industry was advised on 2 April 1976 by a minute, which was received by a Mr Wallace, which outlined all of these ingredients which together go to make up a breach of our GATT treaty obligations. I will quote exactly what Mr Wallace said subsequently in his evidence to the Senate Committee. He stated:
I did not tie them together at that time.
The Minister for Communications invokes the competence of both Mr O'Bryan and Mr Wallace as witnesses to verify that he would not have known, did not know, and could not have known of the circumstances of this breach. Here are two people who are highly skilled, one presumes; highly professionalised. They are certainly highly placed in the Department of Primary Industry, specialists in their field, presumably, who are present when discussions are taking place wherein the ingredients are presented which make it clear that we are breaching our GATT treaty obligations. Even a person with a fairly fundamental understanding of the GATT treaty obligations would have immediately apprehended the nature of the breach being committed. I find it hard to believe, to be quite blunt about it, that from April 1976 to late 1976 neither of these people and no one in the Department of Primary Industry ever understood that the evidence clearly before them culminated in evidence of a breach of our treaty obligations. The Department said in its evidence in May 1981 that no minutes or documents were ever kept documenting or recording discussions between Mr Webster and the Minister for Communications in relation to an oral briefing which Mr Webster swears on oath that he provided for the Minister about the way in which this problem was 'amicably resolved'. Yet last Thursday, in this House, the Minister for Primary Industry flew in the face of that evidence when-I quote him exactly-he said:
The conclusions-
Of the Committee:
are contrary also to all the minutes and documentation that were available to the Department of Primary Industry.
The best source of response to that, of course, is Senator Peter Rae. He gave the lie direct to what the Minister was saying. The following day he said:
These statements of Mr Sinclair are extraordinary when placed against the actual evidence given to the Committee by the Department of Primary Industry who stated that there was nothing on Departmental files which related to the matter in question,
The Departmental evidence' was that there were no relevant minutes, notes, correspondence or documents.
He went on:

1576 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

If such evidence is available as is now claimed by Mr Sinclair then the evidence given to the Committee was untrue-in fact it would amount to perjury.
If the sworn evidence to the Committee was true then it is Mr Sinclair who has made untrue statements to the House of Representatives and to the press conference.
Nothing could be more direct than that. That is the lie direct to the Minister for Communications on the second occasion. But Senator Rae does not stop there. This morning, on the radio program AM, he was interviewed again on exactly the same matter. He said:
Well, if Mr Sinclair is not able to produce the documents, the written evidence which he now says the Committee's finding was contrary to, and I just emphasise that last Thursday to the House of Representatives Mr Sinclair said that the Committee's finding was contrary to the written documents, minutes and records, correspondence, available to the Department of Primary Industry; if he can't produce those now, he misled the House last Thursday.
That is another grave charge against the Minister for Communications. Let us be clear in understanding what is transpiring here. This is not a petty little tea room spat; it is not a squabble between a couple of friends. The implications are large and portentious in their dimensions. This is a very serious confrontation between a Senate committee and the Government. It represents a very serious confrontation between those members of this Parliament who believe in integrity in office, in discharging responsibility properly and adequately, and in being candid and consistent to the Parliament, and a Government that is determined to cover up once again and try brazenly to get away with it. Public comment is properly severe. The Sydney Morning Herald last Saturday stated:
However, for all the furore over this fracas, it involves only one aspect of what has assumed the proportions of a major scandal. This relates to the grave administrative deficiencies within the Department of Primary Industry reaching up to ministerial level. The evidence of laxness by the department in failing to exercise proper supervision of the ADC and to detect glaring abuses within it occurred when Mr Sinclair was Minister.
It went on:
The clear inference from this expanding chronicle ofapparent ineptitude by the department is that something is disturbingly amiss within it, and that Mr Sinclair and Mr Nixon, as Ministers presiding over the department's failings, should bear some responsibility for them.
It concludes:
The revelations of its inadequacies during the terms of Mr Sinclair and Mr Nixon have left its reputation under a dense and noxious cloud. Who or what is to blame? Mr Sinclair and Mr Nixon should come forward with satisfactory explanations . . . this is not an issue to be left up in the air until it blows away. Country Party Ministers are close political cronies of the Prime Minister. In the final analysis it is up to the Prime Minister to ensure that the Government enjoys public confidence.

The Government will not while this sort of preferential treatment is meted out to members of the National Country Party. The Senate Standing Committee not only demolished the veracity of Mr Sinclair, the Minister for Communications, but also accused him of something extraordinarily serious. By implication it accused him of misleading this Parliament-that is, of committing a grave contempt-because the Minister for Communications, while refusing to appear before the Committee to give sworn evidence, stood in this Parliament and made a declaration about his knowledge of events in relation to the Asia Dairy Industries problem. He said then that he saw that declaration as tantamount to a statement on his honour. I leave it to honourable members to make a judgment as to what value there is in the honour of the Minister for Communications.
I remind honourable Liberal Party members of the coalition that Senator Withers went out of office on the basis of a telephone call, a relatively minor transgression and impropriety. The honourable member for Fadden went from his lofty office as Assistant Whip for belling the cat, for telling people that something crooked had gone on in Queensland in relation to a redistribution. But the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) and his predecessor, who is now the Minister for Communications, are guilty of outrageous behaviour in their administration of their departments, their general display of incompetence and the way in which one of them, the Minister for Communications in particular, has been declared by his colleagues from the coalition Government as a person whose word cannot be believed. The Government standards are squalid, self-seeking and offensive to decent-minded people.
Let me remind honourable members of the lofty declarations of the Prime Minister in one of his earlier statements just after the 1975 elections. He said:
We commit ourselves to responsibility in the management of the nation's affairs.
We commit ourselves to integrity in government of the kind that Australia has a right to expect.
It would have been more accurate if he had said 'of a kind that the country had never known before and would wish that it had never known'. When he sacked a colleague, Senator Withers, who clearly took the rap for him and a couple of his colleagues, on 15 August 1978 he said:
We had no doubt that our first and foremost responsibility was the maintenance of the high standards of propriety set and maintained by this Government.

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1577

Fourteen sackings, dismissals and forced resignations in six years-certainly distinctive standards, although I dispute whether they were high. Referring to a statement that he made on 8 August after saying how sad he was and how much it hurt to sack Senator Withers, he said:
The community rightly demands a high standard from the Ministers of the Government.
Mr Scott-When are we going to get it?
Mr Morris-When are we going to get it?
Mr HAYDEN-Exactly. He continued:
The judgments on Ministers are more exacting and sometimes more harsh than the judgments which might be passed on those outside the sphere of public life.
If he believes that, he is yet to put it into practice where the Country Party is concerned. He continued:
If these standards were not upheld, the people's confidence in Government-a confidence which is fundamental to Australian democracy-would be undermined.
He concluded:
The Government has an obligation to uphold them even though the cost can be and is in this instance a high one.
Why does the Government not uphold those standards now? The facts cannot be ignored. The Minister for Communications and the Minister for Primary Industry have imperilled this country's export markets. They have done unparalleled damage to Australia's international reputation. On the evidence available to us, they have been lazy and inattentive in their administrative responsibilities. They have politicised the Department of Primary Industry so that now it is just one more link in the chain of operatives serving the National Country Party constituency.
The Minister for Communications, on the judgmentof his peers from his own party, cannot be believed. Both Ministers have tolerated an extraordinary level of gross inefficiency and stark incompetence when they have been in charge of the Department of Primary Industry. Of course, the defence will be abuse and bluster. There will be personal attacks but there will be nothing which will attend to the central core of these complaints. The Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) has started already. He says that the report is prejudiced and that the members of the Committee are prejudiced.
Even if he were correct-we dispute that on the evidence before us-he is, by terminating his observation there, still accepting a mighty condemnation of the administrative incompetence of the Minister for Communications and the grubby indolence of the Department of Primary Industry. The only prejudices are those against the facts, and those prejudices are harboured by the

National Country Party. The National Country Party will try to tough it out. It has no sense of shame and no respect for the Parliament or the public. If a Liberal member of parliament were involved he would have been out of office weeks ago. He would have been out with less ceremony than that which attended the departure from office of Senator Withers. We know who runs this country under the Fraser Government-a rich grazier from the western districts of Victoria, a Victorian dairy farmer, a northern rivers pig farmer and a hobby farmer from New I;:ngland. So much for the so-called businessman's government.
The Minister for Communications believes nothing succeeds like excesses. He is determined to die a political death of a thousand self-inflicted cuts. Let us look at the Australian Dairy Corporation under his administration-a commercial vehicle careering out of control. It had a weak, selfserving administration which he never once sought to upbraid, to give direction to, or to ensure that it fulfilled its responsibilities properly. It went its way-all over the place---:and as long as it kept away from him he was happy.
Let us look at the findings of the Senate Committee report. The Committee found that the Corporation had breached international treaty obligations, its statutory authority and its articles of association. The report also stated that it improperly engaged in tax avoidance on an international scale. No wonder the Government is always reluctant to do anything about tax avoidance in this country. The Committee found that the Corporation engaged in improper rebate arrangements, that there was evidence of a conflict of interest, that there were slack expenditure controls and unauthorised payments. The Committee found that millions of dollars are missing which are unaccounted for. All this has occurred under the 'distinguished'-I use inverted commas to ensure that the irony is not missedadministrative skills of the hobby farmer from New England, masquerading as the Minister for Primary Industry on that occasion. This is more like the record of a shady one dollar company front conducted by corporate pirates than a commercial flag carrier drawing on the prestige of this country to conduct its affairs. The evidence is that the two Ministers-the Minister for Communications and the Minister for Primary Industryknew of the material facts and have sought over an extended period to submerge as much as they could the hard evidence and the concrete justification for widespread national concern on this matter coming before the Parliament.

1578 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

Let me return to a point I made earlier. It is clear that the defence of the Minister will be: 'Obviously there have been blunders; I knew nothing about them'. I do not believe him on the basis of the evidence which I see before me, on the considered conclusions of the Senate Committee in its report and on the basis of the rather prevaricating approach of some of the witnesses from the Department of Primary Industry in responding to cross-examination before that Committee. I do not believe, as bad as I think his incompetence is, that it is so excessive that he could not have known for the extended period that he claims not to have known about this serious treaty breach. I do not believe that, as deficient as I think his attention is to detail, he would have neglected to follow up such a vague letter as that which came to him from Mr Webster declaring that matters had been settled amicably. He will say: 'It is a blunder'. He will say that he knew nothing about it. Let me quote what the Prime Minister used to believe about this. This extract was contained in a publication entitled The Australian Institute of Political Science; Proceedings of 38th Summer School-29-31 January 1972'. This goes right to the heart of this issue. The Prime Minister said:
But if a major blunder occurred in a department, of real importance to Australia, and if the minister were unaware of it, the minister should accept responsibility for the mistakes of his department and offer to resign.
I throw it back in the teeth of the Prime Minister. He should stand by the declarations of principle that he enunciated so loftily then. Or is it that the indulgences are so free and easy for the National Country Party and that the tough measures are for those outside the Fraser faction in the Liberal Party? Look at the attempt by the Minister for Primary Industry at concealment. He embarked in this Parliament on a prolonged and unsubtle campaign to cover up. For more than two years now he has had the Auditor-General's report on this matter. Look at the defences he has hopped on-from one to the other. He said: 'It would be delicate and commercially sensitive to release it'. When that did not wash, he said: 'It would not be proper for it to be released'. Then he said that the Auditor-General would not allow the report to be released. But that was refuted by a representative of the Office before a Senate Committee. Finding that that would not hang, he said that the police were handling the affair. Then he said, most outrageously, on 15 November 1979 that matters had been rectified. This report makes it clear that matters are still outstanding, require rectification, have not been attended to, in spite of the Minister's misleading the Parliament. He promised that there would be a statement in the autumn session of 1980, and he never delivered it. On 10 June this

year he said that he hoped to make a statement that day. The fact is that he has tried to keep it on the back burners to keep it away from public attention. We have in the Minister for Primary Industry and the 'Minister for Communications, indisputably two tough politicians but two totally incompetent public administrators, and that is what counts. The Department of Primary Industry, on the record of this report, is undeniably incompetent. It has been drifting along oblivious to what has been happening and indifferent to its responsibilities. In all of that there has been a complicity by the Minister. Page 176 of the report states:
Summarising our views here, we consider that such Ministerial involvement in the ADC should not be by way of a 'request' (which does not become known to the parliaf11ent or the public). He should give a formal direction which is recorded in writing and brought to the attention of the Parliament as soon as possible notwithstanding that this will disclose Australia's breach of a treaty. We also believe that there is an obligation on the Minister to ensure that a direction is complied with once it is given. Ministerial inaction in the face of a known continuing breach cannot be condoned.
That is the charge by clear implication of the Senate Committee-ministerial condoning of a breach by inaction.
Mr Speaker, there are other matters to be raised and they will be raised by other speakers from this side of the House. I conclude on this point: There is one key question to be asked in this affair. It is a question which this Government and this Prime Minister refuse to face up to. It was asked by the Canberra Times only two days ago in a leading article on the subject of ministerial responsibility, a subject which this Government does not want to recognise. The question is:
What does a Minister, or a political party, a Government or a government department have to do before the person or organisation is held responsible for things which should never have happened? Responsible, that is, to the point of sackings or resignations, removal of or restrictions on responsibilities, even portfolios? Responsible, too, for actually, finally, eventually, admitting to Parliament and the people what went wrong, and why, and how and when?
That is the question. That is the real issue now before the Parliament. It is a question made more pertinent by the very promises, the pledges, given by this Prime Minister to the Australian people six years ago and by the very grave abuse of the standards of integrity which he declared so loftily, grave abuse which has quite evidently been committed by the Minister for Communications in association with the Minister for Primary Industry.
Mr SPEAKER-Is the motion seconded?
Mr HAWKE (Wills) (2.24)-1 second the motion moved by the Leader of the Opposition

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1579

(Mr Hayden). In particular, I shall direct my remarks to the activities of the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) and the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to sustain the motion moved by the Leader of the Opposition. I have had occasion before to inform this House and to prove to it that the Minister for Industrial Relations is guilty of deliberately misleading it. The Minister's euphemism for his action on that occasion was to say:
It might fairly be said that my. answer last week contained an error of fact.
The Minister has again treated this House with contempt, but on this occasion in circumstances infinitely more damning and deserving of censure by this House. Let us come straight to the Minister's performance in this House last week. At the outset, let us get this point perfectly clear: All the public criticism of the Minister for Industrial Relations since his performance last Tuesday has simply been in terms of his incompetence. The suggestion that has been made publicly and widely is that the Minister made a blunder. Indeed, he did make a blunder, but much more important than the fact that he made a blunder is this: The Minister lied to the House; and he knows that he lied to the House; and even more importantly the Prime Minister of this country knows that his Minister for Industrial Relations lied to this House.
The genesis of this extraordinary performance goes back to the events in House of Representatives Estimates Committee E on the preceding Tuesday evening of 8 September. At that meeting of the Estimates Committee the Minister for Industrial Relations was very rigorously questioned about his handling of the preparation of the application for the deregistration of the Builders Labourers Federation. That questioning, as a matter of record, was predominantly directed by me and by the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock). The Minister did not fare well in that episode. In the proceedings before the Estimates Committee on that evening of Tuesday, 8 September, the Minister's criticism of the lack of preparedness of his predecessor and the promise of the present Minister for Industrial Relations to expedite deregistration proceedings were contrasted with the fact that then, in September, no application had been made or was likely to be made before next year and the fact that to that stage at least $750,000 had been spent on or committed to that exercise in this financial year.
As I have said, the Minister for Industrial Relations did not fare well in those proceedings before the Estimates Committee on the night of Tuesday, 8 September. But the Minister is the sort

of man who could not leave it there. He was obviously upset by the grilling that he had received from the honourable member for Kooyong and me. So what did the Minister do?
It is vital that every member of this House understand the sequence of events after the night of 8 September. The Minister was concerned about what happened. On the following Tuesday he raised in the Cabinet room, with people including the Prime Minister, what in fact had happened at the Estimates Committee on the previous Tuesday night. A decision was made then, collusively between the Prime Minister of this country and the Minister for Industrial Relations, that they would get the honourable member for Kooyong, the previous Minister for Industrial Relations. And how they would get him? They collusively decided that a Dorothy Dix question on this issue~the forms of this House would be usedwould be directed by a member from the Government side of the House to the Minister for Industrial Relations. I remind the House of precisely what happened. On that day the honourable member for Kingston (Mr Chapman) directed a question to the Minister for Industrial Relations. This is the concocted, collusive perversion of the forms of this House, collusively arrived at between the Minister for Industrial Relations and the Prime Minister. This question was directed to the Minister:
Is the Minister for Industrial Relations aware of recent reports which suggest delay by the Government in taking action against the Builders Labourers Federation? What progress has been made in preparation of the case for deregistration?
The Minister's answer, which will be well remembered I am sure, went in these terms:
I have seen reports of the kind referred to by the honourable gentleman. Last week, on 8 September, in Estimates Committee E some questions were asked of me on this subject.
Were they ever! The Minister's answer continued:
Because of these developments, since last week I have examined the file from its-inception.
We will come to that file in a moment. He said he had examined the file from its inception and he went on to say:
Therefore I think it is appropriate that I place some facts on the record so as to show the circumstances surrounding preparation of the case and the state of unpreparedness when I assumed responsibility for the portfolio.
I re-emphasise to every honourable member of this House what was happening. There was a Dorothy Dix question and then the Minister came in and said: 'I am going to put the facts before the House so that I can give to the House the, information concerning the state of unpreparedness when I assumed responsibility for the portfolio'. Honourable members will recall that he then

1580 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

started to quote from a memorandum from the Senior Assistant Crown Solicitor to the Attorney General (Senator Durack). Of course, he s~arted off by quoting the memorandum which stated:
Progress in the preparation of the case has been very
slow . . .
He had not got very much further when the honourable member for Melbourne Ports (Mr Holding) asked that he table the document. The Minister proceeded to table that document. Of course, honourable members did not have the document before them at that stage. It is very interesting to note that what he went on to quote was extraordinarily selective. It is of interest, if one looks at this memorandum from the Senior Assistant Crown Solicitor to the Attorney General, to see what sentence comes after the sentence which the Minister for Industrial Relations had quoted. He had quoted: 'Progress in the preparation of the case has been very slow'. The memorandum went on to state:
The principal reason for this has been the delay in support from the private building industry. You will recall' that the industry sought and obtained certain assurances from the Government to protect companies from loss due to industrial action after de-registration.
Of course, the Minister did not quote what was the primary reason for the delay; he simply went to the fact that it was slow. Having done that he went on to rubbish the honourable member for Kooyong because of the state of preparedness and the lack of preparation that he, the Minister for Industrial Relations, asserted he had found when he took over on 16 April this year from the honourable member for Kooyong. He proceeded to rubbish him and to contrast that lack of preparedness which he alleged he found when he took over the portfolio with the action which he initiated after he assumed responsibility. As I said, he did not read the whole of the document. Of course, as we know, and what all the emphasis has been upon in the document, reference was made to one particular company-Costain Australia Ltd. We all know what happened as a result of the tabling of that document by the Minister, involving as it did an absolute breach of the basic undertakings that have been given by the Government to employers in the construction industry that there would be no disclosure because they feared retributive action by this organisation. I and the Opposition come in here with no brief for that organisation nor do we condone or support its action. The Department, the Ministers and the Government knew that if there was any disclosure there could be that sort of action and an absolute binding undertaking had been given by the Government that there would be no naming of people before the situation had been reached in

the court when everyone would be named. We have all that problem involved in the undertakings being broken.
All that is as nothing to the point to which I come now. What I now reveal to this House should lead every honourable member of this House to say that he can no longer tolerate the lack of integrity involved in the activities of the Minister for Industrial Relations and the collusive involvement of the Prime Minister with those actions. The Minister knows that what he attempted to convey to this House on Tuesday of last week about the alleged lack of preparedness in the Department when he came into that Department after 16 April was precisely the opposite of what he, as Minister, had conveyed in a written memorandum to the Cabinet at the beginning of May this year.At the beginning of May 1981, just after he had taken over the portfolio, the Minister submitted to the Cabinet a written memorandum which covered a report of the Department of Industrial Relations for the month of April. In that written memorandum to the Cabinet in which, in part, he dealt with the question of what stage had been reached in the preparation of the application for the deregistration of the Builders Labourers Federation, the Minister for Industrial Relations expressed great satisfaction with the state of preparedness he found in the Department on this matter of the application for deregistration. He expressed great satisfaction about the extent of the progress that had been made and the quality of the preparation that had been undertaken. That was the written report on this issue to the Cabinet at the beginning of May. That was the considered report of the Minister for Industrial Relations undertaking his obligation to report to Cabinet in writing about the state of play; and he expressed great satisfaction about the extent of progress and the quality of preparation.
Such is the utter contempt in which this Minister holds this House that when, in collusion with the Prime Minister, he seeks to use the forms of this House to pursue a personal intra-party vendetta, he will convey to this House, over which you preside, Mr Speaker, the exact opposite of the true position he has earlier conveyed to Cabinet. I ask the Minister to note what I now say. The Minister knows that what I am saying is the truth, as does the Prime Minister. I challenge the Minister for Industrial Relations, when he replies in this debate, to refute what I have been saying by producing his written report to the Cabinet of early May this year covering his report on the Department of Industrial Relations and specifically

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1581

covering the state of preparation of the prosecution in respect of the application for deregistration, particularly in terms of the expression of his attitude of satisfaction or dissatisfaction on the state of progress and the quality of preparation. I challenge the Minister to deny what I am saying-to refute what I am putting-by producing that written document.
I ask honourable members of this House: What more do they want as grounds for censure of a Minister and of a government than that the Minister, collusively with the Prime Minister, will tell the truth to Cabinet and will not tell the truth to this House; will in fact lie to this House, and will do that for the naked purpose of pursuing. a vendetta-I repeat, collusively with the Prime Minister-against a member of their own party for what they perceive as their advantage in pursuing a dangerous opponent? I put it to you, Mr Speaker, that if members of this House will tolerate this, they will tolerate anything. If they will tolerate this delinquent deception whereby the Cabinet is told the truth and they, as members of the House, are told lies, they put themselves just as much in contempt of this House as do the Minister and the Prime Minister.
Let there be no doubt about what is involved here. We are not simply dealing with the Minister for Industrial Relations telling lies to this House, telling the opposite to the truth he has told the Cabinet. What we are facing is a situation where that has been done with the collusion of the Prime Minister. The Prime Minister and the Minister for Industrial Relations were aware of the truth in the Cabinet minute of early May 1981. The Minister said to the House last Tuesday: 'I have looked at the file from its inception.' The file from its inception obviously contains the report to the Cabinet. In that report to the Cabinet, the Minister unqualifiedly expressed his satisfaction with the extent of progress and the quality of the preparation of the case that has been taken. What do they do? The Minister then gets together with the Prime Minister in the Cabinet room and the conversation goes like this: 'Look, the honourable member for Kooyong gave us a bit of a rubbishing the other night. We are also getting somewhat worried about where he may be getting in the numbers game. So what will we do? We will go into the House of Representatives, misuse its forms by having a question put and you, the Minister for Industrial Relations, will tell the House of Representatives the opposite of, in fact, what you have put to the Cabinet in that memorandum'. If the Prime Minister of this country is going to be involved in that collusive practice to

get the Minister to tell lies to the House of Representatives in absolute contradiction of the written memorandum which he has put to the Cabinet, where is the integrity of this Government? It simply does not exist.
I ask honourable members to dwell upon the frequency of this situation. What is the context in which this matter has arisen? The Minister for Industrial Relations and the Prime Minister have said: 'We demand of the trade union movement standards of behaviour. We cannot tolerate the Builders Labourers Federation because it does not keep .its word. It breaks agreements. Its members do not act honourably'. That is what this is about. The Government says: 'Because the Builders Labourers Federation does not act honourably, does not abide by its agreements and has double standards of behaviour, we, the Government, from our position of moral indignation against this organisation say: "We will do two things. We will proceed to apply for its deregistration and we will also have a royal commission into its activities.".' It is these people who say that those are their standards and, at the same time as they appear to set those standards or purport to set those standards for others, what are they doing? They are conniving collusively in the Cabinet room of this country to ensure that the forms of this House will be deliberately used to tell lies to the House of Representatives. The truth is on the record.
I repeat my challenge to the Minister for Industrial Relations: When he gets up to reply in this debate, I challenge him to produce his written memorandum. That is where the truth is. If that record is produced, it will show that he expressed his unqualified satisfaction. But he got up then to talk about the state of unpreparedness. He did that a week later after the Estimates Committee hearings.
Mr Speaker and honourable members of this House, we have reached a position, as the Leader of the Opposition said, where there is no integrity in this Government and there is no integrity in the Prime Minister. But, worse than that, there is this sickening hypocrisy which spends millions of dollars to impose penalties upon trade unions because it is said that those unions are not abiding by proper standards of behaviour when these people are lying collusively to this House of Representatives. They lack integrity. They should resign.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER (Wannon-Prime Minister) (2.45)-1 will deal with these matters in the order in which they were dealt with by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) and the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke). I

1582 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

would like to start with some comments about the work of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations because it is obviously on that report that the Leader of the Opposition rested his case. The Committee has produced a wide-ranging report on a considerable
number of matters concerning the Australian Dairy Corporation. The report covers matters of management, it raises issues about the subsidiaries of the ADC as well as the ADC itself, and it considers the broader issue of the relations between the Australian Dairy Corporation and bodies like it and the Government. The Committee has gone right back to the establishment of the Australian Dairy Corporation in July 1975.
Honourable members will know that action has already been taken in relation to a large number of points raised in the report. Indeed, I should point out that it was the actions of the former Minister for Primary Industry that led to the Audit Act being changed so that there could be an Auditor-General's report on this matter. He referred many matters in relation to the Australian Dairy Corporation to the Auditor-General in March of 1979. There has been more than one re-
port. There have been police investigations as a consequence. I think it is worth noting that it was not until November 1980 that the Senate Standing Committee began its inquiries. So the point that needs to be noted by the House is that the Government acted well before the Senate Standing Committee in matters that required action, action is still in train and the Minister is at this moment waiting for what hopefully will be a final police report in relation to these matters.
As to the body of the report, the Government will be responding to the Senate on the substance of it and doing it in the proper way. But of all the aspects of the report, this motion concentrates on one aspect and on one aspect alone. I want to refer to that and deal with it. The only issue before the House today in this matter is whether there was a conversation on some unspecified date in November or December 1976, at some unspecified place, providing some unspecified details at which the former Minister for Primary Industry was told by Mr Webster that an arrangement was in operation which involved a continuing breach under the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade. Whether he was so told is a question that has to be answered wholly on the basis of the recollections of the people involvedwholly, in fact, on the basis ofMr Webster's recollections alone, because no other evidence has been presented anywhere in the Senate Committee's report or, as far as I am aware, outside the Senate Committee's report which supports the view that

the conversation took place in which Mr Webster, despite his letter to the Minister, indicated to the Minister that there was a continuing breach of the GATT arrangement.
The Senate Committee has come to the conclusion that on the balance of probabilities Mr Sinclair's recollection of the events was not as reliable as that of Mr Webster. But it is important for honourable members to remember that the Senate Committee made no suggestion that any-
one had any intention to mislead. The report does not challenge the genuineness of the Minister's recollections; nor has the Senate Committee made any recommendation arising out of its conclusion. It needs to be made plain from the start that neither the evidence on which the conclusion was based nor the conclusion itself would have provided any foundation for a recommendation. But, while there is no recommendation from the Senate Committee, the House needs to look closely at the evidence. While there is no question about the integrity of the Committee, it is ob-
viously the responsibility of this House to reach its own conclusions about the evidence and the recollections of the people concerned. The starting point has to be Mr Webster's recollections because there is nothing else, apart from his recollections, which indicates in any way whatever that the conversation took place in which Mr Webster had indicated to the Minister that there was a continuing breach of GATT, a conversation which, if
it had taken place, would have had to contradict his letter which had earlier gone to the Minister as a result of that Philippines meeting.
I will first submit to the House an analysis which shows that Mr Webster's evidence does not
provide a sufficient basis for reaching the conclusion that he told the Minister of the breach. Secondly, I shall submit to the House that the other evidence which is available strongly supports the Minister's account of the matter. It is, of course, not in doubt that when the Minister was
initially informed by Mr Webster that an arrangement was in force which breached GATT he gave instructions that the arrangement must cease. Nor is it in doubt that after a special trip to the Philippines undertaken to terminate the arrangement Mr Webster informed the Minister in a letter of 19 November 1976 that an amicable solution to our outstanding difficulties had been arrived at. Nor is there any doubt that the only interpretation which the Minister could conceivably put on those words was that the breach had been overcome.
It is here that we come to Mr Webster's statement and his evidence before the Senate Committee. The details were given orally; that is, the

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1583

details about the solution of which Mr Webster had advised the Minister in his letter. The inescapable point is that in his evidence Mr Webster did not, at any point, say what the details were, not did he eVer actually say in his evidence that he told the Minister that the GATT minimum was being breached. Mr Webster was given six or seven opportunities, some of them quite sharp prods by senators' questions, to say that he told the Minister that the GATT minimum was being breached; but he did not once use those words. He said that he advised the Minister of the facts, but he did not say what the facts were. He was then asked: 'Was there.any conversation between you about the implications of the continued breach of GATT?', to which his reply was: 'I could not honestly recollect specifically'-that is a wonder~ ful collection of words-'what the conversation was. I can clearly'remember that I reported'the facts'. Again, words overladen with doubt. If he had told the Minister it i& inconceivable that they would not have discussed the implications. Yet Mr Webster says he could not honestly'recollect specifically what the' conversation' was; Senator Evans, apparently disappointed with this reply, asked: 'You cannot recollect?'. M( Webster replied: 'Not the details. I know that the problem must'-I ask the House to note that word 'must'-'have been defined '!i.'nd the basis of a solution determined and that was made available'. The word 'rn'ust' in this reply is a significant word indeed. It is the word used by a person who does not know or recollect what was said but has come to a separate and later conclusion. It is hard not to think that this answer is an account not of what he did say to the Minister but rather of what he ought to have said but did not say to the Minister.
Mr Webster had more to say, which has a significant bearing indeed on the reliability qf his recollections. It is commort for a notetaker to be present at discussions with Ministers. It is not surprising that Mr Webster was asked whether a notetaker was' present on this' occasion. He-was asked: 'Do you know if any officer of the Department of Primary Industry was present during that discussion?', to which he replied: 'I think so, but I would not be categoric'. lie thought, arid he was not categoric. His recollection is that somebody from ,the Department was present He' was then asked: 'Was thatMr Wallace or Mr a'Bryan?'. He replied: 'Either one or the other,but I would not be categoric on that'. He, is not a very categoric person. He is not a very certain person in the evidence that he provides. Mr a'Bryan has said categorically that he was not present and the Minister accepted that.

I would like the House to note at a later point that the Committee found Mr a'Bryan a creditworthy witness because there were other things that Mr a'Bryan said which are relevant to this particular matter but which, on my reading, were not in fact contained in the report, but they are in the evidence. Mr Wallace also has said categorically that he was not present, but because of the basis of some of the questioning and answers the Committee wanted to qualify that. So be it. But even if it is so qualified-which I do not accept, but the Committee did-the very strong probability is that neither Mr Wallace nor Mr a'Bryan was present at a meeting at which a Minister was told of a continuing GATT breach. The strong probability that Mr Webster's recollection is incorrect on whether an officer of the Department was present is further confirmed by the fact that there is no departmental note. If an officer had been present, surely there would have been a note of the conversation; certainly there would have been on an important matter.
In addition to the doubts that must be in anyone's mind, on a simple analysis of Mr Webster's recollections other significant factors need to be taken into account. There is the evidence of the Minister which clearly states that he had no recollection of a meeting and which states categorically that 'certainly no evidence was presented to me~-that is, the Minister-'to indicate that the ADC's share of the credit was being used in breach of the GATT minimum price'. There ought to be no doubt in this place about the importance of a statement made in the Parliament by a Minister or any member of parliament. The Minister's account is therefore supported by the evidence of Mr a'Bryan and Mr Wallace but there'is also further significant evidence that poi'nts the same way. It strongly supports the view that the conversation in which the Minister is alleged to have been told of a GATT breach-even though Mr Webster never used the words in reporting or talking of that conversation between himself and the Minister-did not take place at all. There iS'evidence on page 186 of the Hansard transcript of the, Committee's meetings which, I regret, seems to have been overlooked in the report,and is not part of the report as I am advised. This evidence seems to me to be significant indeed. It has major relevance to the reliability of MrWebster's recollections and whether he told the Minister that the GATT minimum price was still being breached. Mr Norwood, General Manager of the Australian Dairy Corporation, in his evidence to the Committee said:
I should like to make one other point which may help you to
understand this information flow. There is no record in official minutes to show th~t the matter was discuss~d by the ADC

1584 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

board; there are, however, references in other documents to discussions which took place at board meetings. When this matter came up yesterday, I obviously wanted to check as best I could. I spoke with three members of the board who were also on the board of the Corporation at the time. They confirmed that immediately prior to the meeting with the Minister and the meeting with Mr Uytengsu, discussion was held by the board of the Corporation who endorsed the decision that the $25 arrangement must cease. They also confirmed that at the first Board meeting after the return of the party from Manila-I do not remember the date-the Board were advised that the arrangement had been terminated.
In other words, Mr Speaker, orally and apparently without a written record, the Board was told that the breach of the GATT arrangement had, in fact, been terminated. That, therefore, is consistent with the letter which Mr Norwood had written to the Minister which is on the public record.
I ask the House to note that Mr Norwood spoke to these Board members quite directly and found that they had no hestitation in saying that they were advised that the arrangement had been terminated. We must assume that Mr Norwood's evidence is correct. It has not been challenged. It is also worth noting that Mr Norwood said that what the Board had been told, whilst not identical, was certainly the same in meaning as .the advice given to the Minister by letter. The letter left open only one conclusion, that is that the breach had been overcome. I point out again that Mr O'Bryan, whose evidence as contained in the report was accepted by the Committee, wrote to Senator Rae on 22 April 1981. There are some references to this letter in the report, but not, I think, to tbis particular point. Mr O'Bryan, whose evidence in other places and in the record of the report was accepted, in paragraph 34 of tbis letter said:
On my return to duty I was informed of Mr Webster's written advice of 19 November 1976 to the Minister that an amicable solution had been reached with HOMP/. And I indicated at the hearing I interpreted that as meaning that agreement had been reached between Mr Webster and Mr Uytengsu for the withdrawal of the rebate payment.
His interpretation, qui~e plainly, was the same as the interpretation of the Minister, the same as that of any person who had been involved in these matters, and the same as that of any person who had given an instruction to the chairman of the Board to go and do a job and who then gets a letter saying that the matter has been fixed up. There could be only one interpretation of that, and that is the interpretation Mr O'Bryan and the Minister placed on it. The fact that this is consistent with Mr Norwood's evidence in that the Board was also told that the arrangement was terminated is highly significant indeed. I ask all honourable gentlemen to go to the transcript and read that part of the evidence because it helped to put other aspects into balance.

It is worth noting, I think, that the letter left open only one conclusion, that the breach had been overcome. I point out that Mr O'Bryan came to exactly the same view of it. Is not Mr Norwood's evidence further strong evidence that no conversation occurred in which Mr Webster clearly pointed out to the Minister the consequences of continuing a GATT breach? I ask the House: Is it conceivable that Mr Webster would officially tell the Minister one thing, advise the Board of the same thing, but then secretly tell the
Minister something else? Such a proposition is absurd and utterly unreal. If Mr Webster had put himself in that position, he inevitably would be confronted with his own contradiction. Granted that the Minister had been so forthright earlier in saying that if there were a breach it must be overcome, surely Mr Webster would know that if the Minister were advised that the breach was continuing he would report the matter to his Department and seek advice, or speak to his colleague, the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) and seek advice. So whichever way it went, the matter would not have been contained which, if there had been such a conversation, was plainly Mr Webster's intention.
Would a person like Mr Webster put himself in a position where, the first time the Minister and the Board met-as inevitably they would-the Minister would have said to the Board: 'We have a mCl.jor problem. The breach with the Philippines is continuing. What will they do about it?' The Board members would say: 'But didn't Webster report to you? It has been terminated and overcome. It has been fixed up'. Mr Webster would be faced immediately with his now contradiction.
Again these circumstances and this evidence strongly indicate that a discussion with the Minister did not take place, in the terms which have been mentioned, and in which the Minister was advised that there was a continuing GATT breach. If one were to come to such a conclusion, it would be contrary to all the evidence except the shaky recollection of Mr Webster. We are left with that shaky recollection of Mr Webster pointing in one direction, albeit vaguely, and the statements of Mr Sinclair, Mr Wallace, Mr O'Bryan and Mr Norwood collectively pointing very strongly in the other direction. I do not doubt the integrity of the Senate Committee. But I must say that the Government comes to a different conclusion. Plainly, this is a political occasion. I ask honourable members to think calmly for a moment and to consider who in this House would want to be condemned on the basis of the uncertain, unconfirmed recollections of one person about a possible conversation five years ago? If

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1585

that were the basis of condemnation in this House there would be few people uncondemned.
I will finish the point I am making by noting that in the report the Committee came to a conclusion on the balance of probabilities but, in coming to this conclusion, it seems to have overlooked some very considerable improbabilities. Despite the fact that the Minister gave the initial instructions, despite the fact that there was a special trip to Manila to implement them, despite the reassuring terms of Mr Webster's letter to the Minister, despite the fact that Mr Webster's evidence to the Committee implied that he had not carried out the Minister's instructions and had misled the Minister in his letter of 19 November, and despIte the evidence in the opposite direction of the Minister, Mr O'Bryan, Mr Wallace and Mr Norwood, I do not believe that any honourable member of this House would want to be condemned or be asked to resign wholly and solely on the basis of one person's recollections of a conversation five years ago, especially when those recoilations are unconfirmed. If they were equivocal on the central issue, as Mr Webster's recollections were, and if their truth would have meant that Mr Webster knowingly put himself in an impossible situation vis-a-vis his Minister and his board, there is no caSe for any action or resignation. There is no substance in this motion and I reject it totally.
