Mr ANTHONY-The basis for the charges laid by the Opposition is largely the findings of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations and, in particular, one finding which in my view is wrong and cannot be supported. Obviously the Opposition too has some doubts about the situation because it has hedged its bets by roping another Minister into this censure motion. The Opposition is unsure whether to concentrate its efforts on the Senate Committee's report. The Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden), putting aside his vindictiveness, said that the Department of Primary Industry had blundered in its dealings with the Australian Dairy Corporation and its subsidiaries. Let me say this: The Department has not blundered. It if did anything wrong, it was because it was conned by some very clever and very skilled people in the Australian Dairy Corporation who over a period of years were able to hide the facts and divert attention away from what was going on.
The Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) to his credit has recognised the seriousness of some of the situation surrounding this Senate Committee report. But the honourable senators who undertook this hearing seem to have missed the seriousness of some of these points, which were brought out in a letter written by Mr O'Bryan to the Senate Committee. The letter makes the point which the Deputy Leader of the Opposition made: What happenedto the money? Should there not be a clear investigation into this point? Yet the Senate Committee completely overlooked this question. The Government has not suggested for one minute that Mr Webster perjured himself. What we say is that the Senate

Committee was inefficient in its cross examination of Mr Webster to get the facts from
him.
Like the Leader of the Opposition, I intend to speak only about the Senate Committee's report and the implications of it. In the terms of this debate, the central issue raised in this report is whether Mr Webster, when he returned from Manila in November 1976, told Mr Sinclair that the so-called General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade breach was continuing. On this key point, Mr Sinclair has maintained that he was not told that this so-called GATT breach was continuing and that he was told just the opposite. Mr Sinclair's statement was strongly supported by two senior officers of the Department of Primary Industry. On my reading of the Committee's report and the evidence, Mr Webster did not tell Mr Sinclair that the so-called breach was continuing. I take that view not just because the Minister said that Mr Webster did not tell him, but because at no time in his evidence did Mr Webster say that he told the Minister that the so-called breach was continuing. Yet, it is on the Committee's assertion and acceptance that Mr Webster did say that to the Committe that Mr Sinclair is now, so to speak, on trial if he has not, in fact, already been charged guilty by some people.
The House may find my repetition of this point tedious, but I make no apology for that because it is the point of greatest importance and it needs to be understood. The question is whether Mr Sinclair or Mr Webster is to be believed. The Committee says, in fact, that on the balance of probabilities it believes Mr Webster and it does not believe Mr Sinclair. The issue around which these questions revolve is whether Mr Sinclair knew that there was a continuing so-called breach of the GATT after he instructed that that breach must cease. Honourable members will note that I have been using the phrase 'the so-called breach of GATT'. There was, in fact, no breach of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade. There was a breach of what is known as the arrangement concerning certain dairy products. This was an arrangement entered into by a limited number of parties to the GATT, dairy producing nations and administered within the GATT framework. It is unfortunate that, in attempting to find a shorthand description, the words used have tended to convey the impression that the breach related directly to Australia's obligations under a major international treaty.
So, there was a breach of an arrangement under GATT. When Mr Sinclair became aware of it, he told Mr Webster that the breach must cease. He asked Mr Webster, on 9 November 1976, to take

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1591

action to rectify the situation. No one, Mr Webster, the Committee or anyone else-disputes that. Mr Webster went to Manila and held a meeting there on 15 November ostensibly to fix the problem. He wrote to Mr Sinclair four days later saying that an amicable solution to the outstanding difficulties had been reached. Mr Sinclair could only reasonably have taken this to mean that the so-called GATT problem had been solved. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) pointed out a very significant point and that was that Mr Brian Norwood, the general manager of the Australian Dairy Corporation, told the Committee that the board had been told, after the Manila meeting, that the arrangement that had been causing the breach had been terminated. He said that three members of the board had confirmed that the board had been told that the arrangement had been terminated. So here we have Mr Webster writing to Mr Sinclair telling him that the matter had been amicably resolved in Manila and that the Australian Dairy Corporation board had been told by Mr Webster that it had been resolved. Yet, the Senate Committee believes-and asks us to believe-that Mr Webster told Mr Sinclair orally that it had not been resolved.
The Committee believes Mr Webster wrote to Mr Sinclair, told him one thing and then spoke to him and told him something else. The Committee believes Mr Webster told his board one thing and told Mr Sinclair another. Well, I do not believe it.
I do not think anyone with an ordinary capacity for understanding would believe it either. Of course, the truth is that there had not been an amicable solution reached or, indeed, any solution reached at all in Manila. But Mr Webster did not tell Mr Sinclair or, indeed, the board about that. Mr Webster told Mr Sinclair that there was a solution. He told the board the same thing. But there was not, as the evidence before the Committee shows. Mr Norwood told the Committee that there had, in fact, been a violent argument in Manila-a fairly, hectic debate. The Committee found, quite correctly, that the problem which Mr Webster went to Manila to solve was not solved. Yet, the Committee, in the final result, believed what Mr Webster told it rather than what Mr Sinclair told it. In doing so, the Committee made a grave error.
Let us look at some of Mr Webster's evidence and, equally important, let us look at some of the questions Senator Evans asked him. The~e are questions in the course of which Senator Evans quoted Mr Webster's letter to Mr Sinclair, saying, wrongly, that an amicable solution had been reached. Senator Evans asked Mr Webster

whether he knew that the arrangement reached in Manila continued the GATT breach. Mr Webster said that it was a question of degree. He said that he was aware of all the facts. He said that he had to make a commercial judgment. Senator Evans asked Mr Webster whether Mr Sinclair had been made aware that the GATT breach was continuing. Mr Webster did not answer that question directly. He said: 'I advised the Minister of the facts'. What facts? Senator Evans asked Mr Webster if there had been any conversation between him and the Minister about the GATT breach. Mr Webster said that he could not honestly recollect specifically what the conversation was. He said that he could clearly remember that he had reported the facts. Again, I ask: What facts? Senator Evans pressed the point again. Mr Webster said that he could not recollect the details, but the basis of a solution had been determined and that was made available. What was made available? Is this the best that these eminent lawyers could do? One could drive a horse and cart through the evidence--
Mr Barry Jones-I raise a point of order, Mr Speaker. I ask the Deputy Prime Minister to read the exact words and not to paraphrase what the exchange was. It is very critical and very important to the argument.

Mr ANTHONY-In none of his answers did Mr Webster come right out and say that he had told Mr Sinclair that the arrangements reached in Manila meant that the so-called GATT breach still existed. Mr ANTHONY-I table the document. This document shows that Mr Webster, in the course of a general discussion on contracts for rural commodities, referred to the matter which, in the light of the Senate Committee's report, has now assumed such significance. Mr Webster said that a discount of $25 per tonne had been provided on skimmed milk powder sold to the Philippines. He then said that this discount had applied, according to the reported discussion, until recently when the minimum price specified under the GATT arrangement had been reached. The record goes on to show that Mr Webster said that when this occurred-that is, the price fell to the agreed minimum-the Corporation had invoked the majeure clause in the contract in order to discontinue sales to the Philippines at prices which would have been inconsistent with the minimum agreed to under the GATT arrangement.

Let us be clear about the import of these comments. Mr Webster was saying that the problem with the GATT arrangement no longer existed; it had been solved. That was on 13 October 1976. Yet less than a month later he discussed the problem with the Minister, Mr Sinclair, and accepted Mr Sinclair's instruction that it had to be fixed. In other words, on 9 November Mr Webster discussed with Mr Sinclair a problem he had claimed on 13 October no longer existed. On 15 November he was in Manila ostensibly to fix a problem which he said on 13 October had been solved. What Mr Webster said on 13 October to two members of the Trade Development Council obviously was not true. He even told the Senate Committee that he had been to Manila on two occasions before 15 November to try to resolve the problem and had made no progress. Why, then, did he tell the Trade Development Council representatives that the problem had already been solved? This is the man whose word the Senate preferred to that of Mr Sinclair.
The Committee has accepted the uncorroborated word of a man who has been shown to have given the Trade Development Council information that was wrong. It has accepted the word of a man who wrote a letter to Mr Sinclair saying that the GATT arrangement problems had been resolved when they had not been resolved. It accepted the word of a man who told his board that the problem had been solved when it had not been solved. The Committee has accepted the word of a man who resigned from his position with the Dairy Corporation after a series of questions about the Corporation's activities was raised by
the Auditor-General. The Committee has accepted the word of a man whose opinions, given in evidence, about the purchase of motor vehicles were described by the Committee as riddled with inaccuracies, as can be seen on page 346 of the report. Why did the Committee do this? I can only believe that the Senate Committee fell into error through being affected by a form of unconscious prejudice.
Quite clearly two things affected the Committee, upset the Committee and gave it the feeling that it had been slighted. Those two things were, firstly, the refusal of Mr Sinclair to make a statutory declaration and, secondly, the attitude of one of the witnesses before the Committee, Mr Wallace of the Department of Primary Industry.
Mr Sinclair chose to give evidence by means of letters and backed them up with a statement in this House. I believe and the Government believes that this was the proper course for a Minister to take. The Committee takes the view that the oaths of office sworn by a Minister are not

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1593

sufficiently binding to enable it to believe that statements a Minister makes carry the weight of statements given in sworn evidence. The Committee, while recognising the significance of a statement made to Parliament by the Minister, apparently felt that a statement made on such a basis was not good enough in itself.
It seems to me that the Committee sought to place itself above Parliament and to demand of the Minister something which the Parliament itself would not demand. For the Committee, a considered and clear statement made to the Parliament by a Minister of the Crown under the highest compulsion and demands of his office and the oaths he has sworn in full knowledge of the gravity of his responsibilities to this Parliament was not good enough. So the Committee felt slighted. It found itself unable to accept the Minister's word.
The Committee described Mr Wallace's evidence as guarded and qualified and his manner as evasive. I will quote a couple of Mr Wallace's answers when he was asked whether he had been at the meeting between Mr Sinclair and Mr Webster. Mr Wallace said:
No, because there was no meeting.
Is that guarded, qualified or evasive? He also said:
I did not attend any meeting.
Is that guarded, qualified or evasive? I invite the House to compare the directness of these answers by Mr Wallace with the indirectness 'of those answers by Mr Webster. (Extension of time granted). I thank the House for its indulgence. I could go on and quote more of what, to me, is vintage Al Wallace, but all I want to say is that I can understand the Committee being put out a little by Mr Wallace's attitude. I have known Mr Wallace for a long time and I can understand that he might on occasions be a little terse or irritating with people. But I have known Mr Wallace to be an absolutely reliable and dependable public servant. He worked with me for a considerable period and was largely responsible for the work done in bringing forward the Australian Wool Corporation in the 1970s. As a result it seems to me that the Committee formed an opinion of Mr Wallace that coloured its views on the whole matter and influenced its findings. The Committee seemed to find difficulty, too, in a different way with the evidence of Mr John O'Bryan, who appeared before it after having left the Public Service because of ill health and without being able to consult departmental files about matters which took place six or seven years before. What does concern me is that the Committee appears to have placed practically no weight at all on a long and

detailed supplementary statement in writing by Mr O'Bryan after he was able to look back on departmental records.
I take this opportunity to point out that I said earlier today that there was no reference to Mr O'Bryan's letter in the report. I have looked again at the report and I find that there is a reference to one or two matters in Mr O'Bryan's statement. But I remain very concerned at what seems to be a lack of interest by the Committee in this most significant statement. It is a statement that throws a lot of light on many matters, including the fact that a most important reference in the draft minutes of the Australian Dairy Corporation Board meeting to the GATT arrangement breach was deleted in the final version of its minutes. Mr O'Bryan's written statement, which he asked the Committee to include in the public record of evidence, also raises the most important question of what authorisation there was of the November 1976 agreement in Manila between Mr Webster and Mr Uytengsu for the payback of accumulated money in the Singapore bank account by way of rebates against future shipments. This was a point that the Deputy Leader of the Opposition brought out. I should have thought that the Committee would have latched on to it and made it the prime part of its investigation.
However, there does not seem to be much response from the Committee. I have no reason to have the slightest doubt about Mr Wallace's honesty or Mr O'Bryan's integrity. No other reasonable person could have doubts having read the evidence put forward in that report. In fact, that is the key issue put to the Committee. Evidence given by Mr Wallace and Mr O'Bryan was categorical, unqualified and direct, and that evidence supported Mr Sinclair. At the same time, the finding against Mr Sinclair was based on unsupported evidence of Mr Webster about whose role and evidence I leave the House to make a judgment. As a layman it seems to me that the bringing down of such a report by the Senate Committee represents a fundamental breach of individual rights and a serious departure from all the accepted principles of justice.
The Committee's report is s'aid to be one on the Australian Dairy Corporation and its Asian subsidiaries. It is,in fact, a very limited analysis of these bodies. It focuses on certain negative aspects of the managerial practice and defects in legislation and obviously it has detected serious inadequacies. It is silent on many big policy issues such as the appropriateness of the ADC's charter in today's circumstances, whether some of the trading activities of the ADC should be invested

1594 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

in the private sector, whether the Asian subsidiaries should be sold and whether the ADC Board should be restructured. It is especially surprisirtg that the Committee is weak on such issues. It received ample evidence of the all persuasive continuing conflict of interests and objectives of various members of the ADC Board and Asia Dairy Industries (Hong Kong) Ltd. Because of these conflicts of interest it seems to me that there were many occasions for various sectional interests to withhold information, to disguise the facts and to evade questions about the facts. It
seems to me that some parties concerned have, over a long period, become skilled in withholding material facts, in diverting attention and generally
circumventing the possibility of an inquiry ~r interest from the Department of Primary Industry and the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr
Nixon) and hence from this Parliament. In these circumstances, the report fails to give adequate recognition to the problems faced by the Department of Primary Industry in carrying out its responsibilities.
Mr Speaker, I reject the Committee's finding about Mr Sinclair. It is a finding that is not supported by evidence. It is a finding that runs counter to the evidence given by Mr Sinclair and senior departmental officers. It is certainly a finding that is not supported by Mr Webster's evidence. Mr Webster's evidence can hardly be said to stand on firm foundations. It is a finding inspired unconsciously perhaps by the proper refusal of Mr Sinclair to place the Senate Committee above this House. Mr Hayden said that unions sometimes acted without justification and that that deserved to be condemned, but we did not hear from the Leader of the Opposition in these proceedings any condemnation of the kind of action which has led this Government to take steps to prepare a case for the deregistration of that union.
As I said, no one can underestimate the complexity of a case of this kind, the necessity for obtaining detailed and comprehensive affidavits, the necessity to approach the task most meticulously and with great detail because this union has a catalogue of abuses to its credit and has prevented any reasonable order in the building industry being achieved. Standover tactics, bans, the breaking of concrete pours, walk-offs, intimidations and blatent disregard for the decisions of the Arbitration Commission became part and parcel of the everyday scene where the BLF was concerned. It was in the face of the persistence of that kind of action in private industry and from the Commonwealth's own experience with the BLF that it became clear that at some time the BLF would have to be dealt with. Against that background, the task of preparing a case is indeed a most complex and difficult one. It is not one to be undertaken lightly. It is not one which is to be pursued lightly. The case requires the proof of a multiplicity of incidents constituting the total conduct alleged to justify deregistration. Inevitably, the course of such a case in court is long and complicated. For example, in 1973 preparation of the case for deregistration occupied 21 sitting days. It was virtually undefended and it may well have taken three times as long if it was fully defended. It involved the calling of more than 100 witnesses. The case took 18 months to prepare prior to going to trial. The present case spanning the activities of the BLF in four States and the Australian Capital Territory will be at
'21

1600 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

least as long and at least as complex. It has been prepared within six months. If the proceedings were issued forthwith, the time taken through the court's processes would be very lengthy. In preparing for such a case, it is not sufficient simply to receive accounts from employers and others about the actions of the BLF; the evidence has to be presented in a legally admissible form and it demands the fullest co-operation of the employers concerned.
What the Commonwealth has been faced with, and what I have been faced with as Minister since I have undertaken this responsibility, has been an enormous exercise of much greater magnitude than the earlier case and it is one in which the bulk of evidence has had to be gathered from the employers. That material has been assembled. The application, which runs into more than 200 pages, has been prepared. The Commonwealth's application is ready to be filed. I think it is necessary to elaborate on this matter at some length for the House and for the public to understand what is involved in the preparation of a case of this kind for deregistration. The exercise concerned with preparing the application has been carried out in a most studied, deliberate and businesslike way. Government, industry and legal resources have been pulled together to get this application assembled. Evidence has come from three areas of activity: Private building contractors, State projects and Commonwealth projects. It has been necessary to establish lines of communications and a range of procedures to handle the flow of information. In the case of private industry this has closely involved industry organisations. To collate, examine, and prepare evidence on Builders Labourers Federation industrial action, a task force, which included officers from the Department of Industrial Relations and the Attorney-General's Department, was established. The work of the task force included interviews with company officials, leading to the preparation of draft statements. These were then settled by the legal officers and then drafted by legal counsel into draft form. The documents were then returned to witnesses for swearing and ultimate presentation in court. More than this, every aspect of the process had been subject to careful checking and repeated reference back to the deponents. It should be remembered that all of this was carried out with employers and others in four States and in the Australian Capital Territory. It has involved the identification of over 450 separate incidents, the interviewing of more than 250 persons and the settling of well over 150 individual statements. It is not surprising, then, that this exhaustive task took six months to complete. It contrasts

with the lead-up time of 18 months for the 1974 deregistration case which the employers mounted. It is not surprising, then, that I was concerned to tell the Estimates Committee that when I took on the responsibility of this office I was concerned to accelerate the preparation of this case and to see that it was prepared as rapidly as possible and brought into court as soon as possible, given the complexity of it, the vastness of the material to be gathered and the magnitute of the task of collating it and putting it into admissible form.
I understand, because word has already been sent into the chamber to me, that the honourable member for Wills has made available to the public the document to which he was referring. I think, therefore, whatever that document says, whatever it is that the honourable member for Wills has already released to the public, that what I have said to this House shows the way in which I have gone about discharging my responsibilities in the preparation of this case.

Mr KEATING (Blaxland) (4.40)-Rarely in this Parliament have we seen a more shabby performance than that of a Minister attacking a fellow colleague, a former Minister, and in the process of so attacking him destroying a company's viability by the actions of the Builders Labourers Federation on Costain Australia Ltd. He came into this chamber today and said in a miserable, weak-gutted speech that what he was really saying was that the case was unprepared for reasons other than ministerial accountability. The Minister is now telling us how difficult it is and how it is now a broader case. But what did he have to say last week? Last week he was talking about the state of unpreparedness when he assumed responsibility for the industrial relations portfolio. He went on to say:
No prior consultation was taken by the then Minister with employer organisations.
One does not talk in this Parliament about a former Minister of one's own party, the Minister of a couple of months earlier, without that being a premediated vilification of that Minister. It was a spiteful vendetta hatched up by the Minister and by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in an attempt to vilify the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock).

Looking at this whole matter today, it is obvious that the answer given last week by this Minister for Industrial Relations was an implication of tardiness on the part of the former Minister, the honourable member for Kooyong, in the matter of the deregistration of the Builders Labourers Federation. Yet when we examine the document of the Attorney-General's Department we find that the former Minister was not in a position to_ get companies to give affidavits because they wished to go through their various employer organisations. The Attorney-General's Department document says that the principal reason for this was the delay in the support from the private building industry. It was not the fault of the honourable member for Kooyong at all. Now, today, we find that advice given by this Minister in May-a couple of weeks after he took over the portfolio-is completely at odds with the advice he gave to this House. Putting it in simple terms, the Minister lied to the House last week, and he has been caught lying to the House.
What do we do with a government which is so dishonourable that the Prime Minister sits it out-I will get on to the former Minister for Primary Industry, the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair), in a few minutes-while we have this outrageous set of circumstances in-which a Minister has been caught red-handed misleading the parliament over a matter as serious as the administration of a former Minister in the same government. Yet the Prime Minister was talking about reprehensible circumstances when two Labor Ministers missed a telex date. He called for their dismissal. That was the reprehensible circumstance that the 1975 general election was called upon, the dissolution of the Parliament, the appeal to the Queen's queen to break up the Par-
liament of Australia so that the right honourable member for Wannon could be Prime Minister of this country. This is the sort of reprehensible circumstance we find today.
Despite all the protestations about propriety in government and how there will be moral standards for Ministers, what have we seen? We have seen the shabbiest, most miserable, weak-gutted attempt by a Minister to try to explain away an answer to a Dorothy Dix question of a week earlier attacking a former colleague, a potential leader of the Minister's own party, to destroy that potential leader's prospects. How could we on this side of the House have any respect for this Minister? How could Costain have any respect for him? How could private industry in Australia have any
respect for this Government? How could this Government have an industrial relations policy when it has a fool such as this as the Minister?

Everybody knew in 1976 that there was a massive over-supply of skim milk powder and casein on the world market. Through the common agricultural policy of the European Economic Community skim milk powder was piled up in Europe and was then released on to the world market. This depressed prices. The Australian Dairy Corporation took the step of giving the $25 a tonne rebate to move the tonnes of milk powder. That was well known. The Minister would have known that. But he came in to say that he never knew this

when, in fact, an officer of the Department of Primary Industry actually sat on the board of the Australian Dairy Corporation. In fact, two officers were on the board over that period-Mr O'Bryan and Mr Wallace of the Australian Dairy Corporation. Of course, a subsidiary of that Corporation was Asia Dairy Industries (Hong Kong) Ltd. What a joke it is to suggest that the Minister did not know that these breaches had taken place. I made that decision-

Senator EVANS-Was the decision to continue those shipments through until June 1977, notwithstanding the breach of GATT, brought to the full knowledge of the Government.

I think the second thing that we need to be aware of is that this whole cause relating to the Asia Dairy Industry report arises because of an alleged breach of GATT. As my colleague the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) said, it is not; in fact, a breach of GATT but a breach of an arrangement entered into under GATT. I think we need to identify the fact-it has been acknowledged at page 138 of the report-that there was a reference by me directly to the Chairman of the Australian Dairy Corporation, immediately a breach of this arrangement under GATT had been identified, requesting that he terminate those arrangements and ensure that the obligations under that arrangement be met. I draw the attention of honourable members of this House to paragraph 9.S on page 138 of the report. The report says:
According to evidence given by Mr Norwood 'The matter was discussed by the Chairman of the Corporation with the Minister for Primary Industry on 9 November . . . On his return to Melbourne after the discussion in Canberra the Chairman reported to me that he had discussed the matter at length with the Minister. He said that the Minister had acknowledged the reason this contract had been entered into with the rebate but pointed out that as the arrangement now breached GATT it must cease.'
I have said that it was the arrangement entered into under GATT that was breached and was not a breach of GATT. I draw the attention of the House similarly to my letter of 16 March 1981 at page 139 of the report which states:
I recall discussing the situation with the then Chairman of the ADC, Mr A. A. S. Webster in November 1976 and requesting him to take action to rectify the situation so that the GATT minimum price would be observed.
Let there be no doubt on anybody's behalf that I, my successor or any member of this Government condone that type of breach, be it an arrangement under GATT or anything else, where it is in breach of an arrangement which we have entered into in the interests of the industry and in accordance with the responsibilities of government. So much for the concept that, in some way, we have condoned the continuation of a breach!
Of course, the balance of the report and the debate this afternoon are centred, rather, on some allegations that have been made and some inferences that have been drawn by the Committee. I think it would be interesting if we had a look at a Press statement issued by the Chairman of the Committee who identified the fact that the Committee pursued its inquiries for a considerable length of time. The Chairman said:
The fact is that the Committee studied the evidence over a period of months, carefully weighed the evidence, heard and saw the witnesses and studied their demeanour. In other words we-
I emphasise the word 'we'-

did all those things which juries around Australia are every day asked to do.
I am a very strong supporter of the parliamentary system of committees. I am worried that the product of the slip-shod way in which this conclusion was reached is that the credibility of parliamentary committees, whether in this place or in the other place, is likely to be threatened. I do not want to go into the details of the number of persons who were present on each of the occasions when evidence was called, but I can mention to honourable members of this place that at no time were any more than four of the members of the Committee present on any occasion when either Mr Webster or Mr Wallace was examined or cross-examined by the Committee. So much for the whole Committee considering the witnesses, their demeanour and so on.
The third point I make is that it is important that we try to identify the role that parliamentary committees should play. We should try to ensure that we support those committees in ways which enable them validly to pursue their proper tasks. Let us not, for one moment, assume that members of committees are capable of examining evidence and coming to conclusions on evidence in circumstances where they assert that they are lawyers but do not practise as lawyers because, in this place, they are practising not as lawyers but as parliamentarians. The whole of the personal explanation that I gave in the House the other day was directed to the fact that there is evidence that has been before that committee-evidence through witnesses, evidence through letters and evidence through minutes-which rebuts the conclusions which have been reached, particularly in respect of me. We need to look and see just what that conclusion was and realise what the Committee is talking about. I refer to paragraph 9.38 on page 174 of the report which, in part, states:
The Committee concludes that on the balance of probabilities,--
I referred to that the other day; I will come back to that-
Mr Sinclair's recollection of the events was not as reliable as that of Mr Webster.
In other words, it is not coming to a conclusion on the events; it is coming to a conclusion on my recollection of the events. I contend, as I did in my personal explanation, that, if one takes the list of letters and memoranda that,have been presented to that Committee and no more, no jury considering the evidence, as the Chairman of the Committee contends, would have reached that conclusion.

he is talking about some other matters relating to the floor price-
we had a mountain of skim milk powder. We knew what the options were in terms of price, that we would be down another couple of hundred dollars a tonne, and we had to make commercial decisions. I made that decision in consultation with my colleagues. I reported it back to the board;

I refer to this aspect because I think it is terribly important to look at where we go. This is an extract of a record of interview with Mr Tony Webster, chairman, and Mr Alan Page, senior marketing officer, Australian Dairy Corporation in Melbourne, 13 October 1976. I will not go through in considerable detail the areas that he canvassed but I suppose it is unfortunate if I do not read this part because it is important. He said:
In the Philippines a discount of $25 per tonne on the world price had been provided until recently when skim milk powder prices reached the minimum specified under the GATT-SMP arrangement. When this occurred the ADC had invoked the Majeure clause in the contract in order to discontinue sales to the Philippines at prices which would have been inconsistent with the GATT minimum.

As a result of this action--
That is, the termination by Mr Webster-
it was likely that Australia would supply half of its expected sales of 25,000 tonnes this year. New Zealand would supply the extra powder which the recombiners needed. This development in the Philippines coincides with a shortage of SMP for export from Australia.

Mr SPENDER (North Sydney) .(5.41)-We have heard this afternoon from the Opposition a lot of talk about a desire to raise honest issues, and to examine facts and much criticism of selective quotations. Perhaps the manner in which members of the Opposition go about their task is best exemplified by the individual who spoke last, that squalid nuisance, the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins) whose tirade represents nothing else than an extravagant exercise in abuse. Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1613

174 of the report of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations
which has already been referred to. What was said then was that it appears to the Committee on the balance of probabilities that Mr Sinclair was told of the terms of the compromise which necessarily involved a continuing breach of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade. In reaching that finding the Committee did not say-as was suggested by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden)-that it found that the Minister was not a believable person. Specifically, it concluded that his recollection of events was not as reliable as Mr Webster's.
In order to come to that conclusion the Committee had to look at evidence because the only basis on which it could determine a question of fact such as that was from the evidence. Therefore, it is necessary to look at the evidence, as some of the honourable members on this side of t~~ House who have spoken have done. OpposItion members referred to selective quotations. That, of course, is the oldest defence of people who do not wish to refer to the evidence because once they do so it does not bear out the assertions that they seek to make. Let us see what the events were. On 2 April 1976 it appears that the full board was informed for the first time of the rebate arrangements. It was on that day that it made a decision that it agreed that the rebate arrangements would have to cease. Honourable members should bear in mind that that decision appears never fo have been reversed. On 9 November 1976-there is no contest about this-the Minister directed that the rebate arrangements would have to cease. I quote from the evidence given by Mr Norwood, the General Manager of Australian Dairy Corporation. He said:
. . . as the arrangement now breached GATT it must cease; the Government could not condone an arrangement which breached GATT.
That is a perfectly plain direction by the Minister to the Chairman as relayed by the Chairman to Mr Norwood, the General Manager. What then happened? A special trip was organised. It was accepted by the Committee that it was, in fact, a special trip. What was its purpose? The Chairman was to go to the Philippines to implement the direction given by the Minister. For those who mightsay, 'Oh well, it was only a request', I point out that when a Minister says that a breach cannot be condoned and must cease, a public servant, a person in Mr Webster's position, would understand plainly what that meant. There is only one meaning and that is, 'You must stop it and that is the direction I am giving you.'
What then happened in this rather extraordinary sequence of events-extraordinary when one

examines the evidence of Chairman Webster, as one must? Mr Webster went to the Philippines and, on 15 November 1976, he signed an agreement. That agreement clearly was a continuing breach of the arrangement which was the subject of the Minister's concern. He went there under directions to remedy the default, to cease breaching the arrangement. What did he do? He entered into an agreement which was quite contrary to the directions given to him by the Minister. As was put to him by Senator Evans, in the course of examination or cross-examination-call it what we will-this was a slightly more indirect way of doing things. What did he do then? He came back and, on 19 November 1976, wrote a letter to the Minister. This letter-to anyone who is willing to take a fairly dispassionate view of the evidence-is of critical importance because it constitutes the only written record, by the person who was at the centre of events, namely, Mr Webster, of what he did when he was in Manila. What did he say? It appears in the examination which was conducted of him. At page 146 of the report he said:
'{ am pleased to advise that it was possible for us to arrive at an amicable solution to our outstanding difficulties' . . .
That statement is referred to also at page 145 of the report where we find the further statement:
. . . some of which were contained in a letter from Mr Uytengsu dated 14 October, of which you have received a copy.
For reasons which I must confess escape me somewhat, the Committee went on to make this comment:
It should be noted that Mr Uytengsu's letter of 14 October referred to above contained no reference to the GATT breach.
That may be so. But what on earth would any sensible person, fairly reading the document think that it meant when this man had been sent to the Philippines for the purpose of giving effect to the Minister's direction? Any reasonable reader could have come to only one conclusion which is: We have arrived at an amicable solution to our outstanding difficulties. One of those difficulties was, of course, the difficulty posed by GATT. In fact, that was the difficulty which sent Mr Webster to Manila. It is ludicrous to suggest that somehow, a sensible, intelligent or reasonable person could write what he wrote and think that he was not saying to the Minister: 'Everything is all right. I have fixed up the problem that I was sent to Manila to fix up'.
That letter, of course, flatly contradicts any suggestion that he later told the Minister something else. Does anyone really think that, having written that letter, he then went to the Minister

1614 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

and said: 'True it is that I wrote that letter. True it is that you sent me to the Philippines to give effect to the direction that you had given to me. But I would like to tell you that, as the Chairman of the ADC, I have deliberately, wilfully and flagrantly disregarded the direction that you gave to me. What I said in my letter was not, in fact, the case. There was no amicable solution. We are still continuing our breach of GATT. Aren't you pleased?'. That statement only has to be examined to show what utter nonsense it is.
Let us then look at some of the evidence which was given to the Senate Committee. If honourable members on the other side of the House would listen, they might inform themselves and they might possibly learn something, although I think it is unlikely to the highest degree. What was Mr Webster's evidence? That is the linchpin. The man upon whom the Committee's report depends contradicts his own letter and his own office because that is what he was sent there to do. Moreover, he has contradicted a standing request, so it seems, of his board made in April of that year, that the rebate arrangement should cease.
Much seems to have been read into the evidence by the Committee. Much of the evidence has been read in this House. I will quote from one or two passages in the Committee report. I do not in any way challenge the integrity of members of the Committee, but I must say that I think they should have applied their minds more to testing and probing the evidence that was placed before them. Let us take Senator Evans's first question to Mr Webster. He asked:
What did you tell the Minister about the outcome of your Manila negotiations on 15 November?
Mr Webster-I told the Minister the basis on which we had negotiated.
Senator Evans should have realised-at least he would have if he had practised in the courts for very much time-that he should have elicited what was the man's recollection. One does not say: 'Very well. You told the Minister the basis on which you negotiated.' What does that mean? It means nothing at all unless we know what that basis is, unless the witness is kind enough to tell us at some time, or unless we have some foreknowledge of the basis on which the negotiations were conducted. But alas, Senator Evans did not persist with this line of questioning. He did not seek to find out precisely what was meant by this rather elliptical statement. He then went on to refer to the letter and, in anothc;r classic example of questioning that does not get an answer, he aid:
That is all that is said in the letter. Was anything more said orally?
Mr Webster~The details were given orally.

A sensible question to follow that would have been: 'What were the details? What did you say?' Of course, it emerged during the course of the questioning, with no thanks perhaps to those who were putting the questions, that Mr Webster could not recollect what was said in any event. Let us take Senator Evans's next question. He asked:
Were you aware that the nature of the arrangement that you settled upon in Manila was conspicuously just as much in continuing breach of the GATT minimum as the previous arrangement, once the Hoor price had been reached?
What was the answer to that question? Was it yes or no? It most certainly was not. The answer was:
Mr Webster-Obviously it was a question of degree.
Stopping there for a moment I must say that that is utter nonsense. It was not a question of degree and never could have been. Mr Webster went on to say:
I was aware of all the facts, yes. I had to make a commercial jUdgment.
That statement is also utter nonsense. He had one thing to do and that was to comply with a directive which had been given to him by the Minister which, plainly enough, he did not comply with.
Let us examine some of the other evidence. This counts against the acceptance of the evidence of Chairman Webster, on the critical point, that is, that in some way he informed the Minister of details which he could not recollect. Mr O'Bryan, the Department of Primary Industry representative on the ADC board, did not corroborate Mr Webster's recollection that somebody was probably there. We can forget about Mr Webster's recollection in that area. Mr Wallace, First Assistant Secretary of the dairy division in the Department of Primary Industry did not corroborate Mr Webster's evidence either. Much has been said of Mr Wallace's evidence. But he denied it in very direct terms. If we turn to Mr Webster's evidence-this is of critical importance-we find that he contradicts himself. In other evidence that he has given we find, on page 148 of the report, that he said again in answer to one of Senator Evans's questions when he was referring to the decision made in the Philippines:
I made that decision in consultation with my colleagues. I reported it back to the board; I reported it to the Minister;
I stress the words 'I reported it back to the board; I reported it to the Minister'. What is the evidence on that? Honourable members heard-the evidence which was read by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) which the Opposition sought to put in doubt. But it is perfectly plain what Mr Norwood's evidence was. Mr Norwood got in touch with three of the directors. They were directors at the time of these events. What did they say? They

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1615

confirmed that immediately prior to the meeting with the Minister and with Mr Uytengsu a discussion was held by the board of the Corporation to endorse the decision that the $25 arrangement must cease, as indeed had been the decision of the board back in April. They also confirmed that at the first board meeting after the return of the party from Manila-I do not remember the date-the board was advised that the arrangement had been terminated. No challenge was made on that evidence that I am aware of. It was allowed to go pass without any adverse comment from the Committee.
What does that amount to? It amounts to this: Three board members informed the General Manager of the time that, when they sat down at the board meeting which took place immediately after Mr Webster came back, they were told simply that the arrangements had been terminated. That is consistent with the Minister's understanding of .the matters; it is consistent with the instructions given to Mr Webster; and it is consistent also with a letter written by Mr Webster. Against this, somehow we are meant to abstract from his evidence sufficient weight to destroy all the adverse evidence and all the adverse criticism that can be brought against his own evidence.
Let me refer very briefly to some of the other things that he said. At page 875 of the transcript, Mr Webster said:
A group of main Board directors knew all the Facts.
This, as I read it, was with reference to the decision in the Philippines. Who were the main board directors? They certainly were not the three Norwood spoke to, and certainly not the person who was on the board as the nominee of the Department. What else did he do? These are all matters that seem to have passed the Committee by when it was examining whether it should accept his evidence. At page 877, the Chairman asked:
Was it not available to all members of the board, as Far as you know?
He was referring to the agreement of 15 November 1976. Mr Webster replied:
My recollection is that I read it at the board meeting rather than that I tabled it.
What stands in the way of that evidence? It is the recollection of the three people who spoke to Mr Norwood and the recollection of Mr O'Bryan. If we look at his evidence we will see that it is perfectly plain that he did not know that there was a continuation of the GATT breach until he heard about it in the course of evidence earlier this year.
If we are to assess the reliability of a witness's

evidence, we have to look to other evidence, evidence that contraverts it, reasons why he may not wish to give an accurate statement, how long ago it was and discrepancies within his own evidence. We have a man who gave evidence which is patently improbable. He is a man who is in conflict with O'Bryan and Wallace as to their attendance at the critical conversation, a conversation of which no record at all exists: He kept none. He is a man who is in conflict with three other board members because he said that he told the board what took place. They say he did not; and the documents are against him. He is a man who is directly in conflict with the only written record made by him at the time; and that is the record that states 'it was possible for us to arrive at an amicable solution to our outstanding difficulties' .
What on earth would anyone think he meant by 'outstanding difficulties'? He was not referring to the sale of sewing machines; he was referring to the reason why the Minister sent him there. On that basis and on the basis of the inherent improbabilities that despite the instructions given to him, despite the specific purpose of the trip, despite the fact that the Minister was the person who initiated his trip, effectively, kicking him along, despite the terms of his letter, despite the fact that he apparently, in fact clearly, stated to directors that the Minister's request had been given effect to and despite the fact that it was a board decision anyway, we are meant to believe that he came back and said to the Minister: 'I had an excellent trip over there. 1 liked the Philippines. Send me up there some other time. I have come to an arrangement which is directly contrary to the instruction you gave me. I hope you will send me away for another holiday'. That is absolute nonsense.
Mr NEWMAN (Bass-Minister for Administrative Services)-I wish to make a personal explanation. I claim to have been misrepresented.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)-The Minister seeks to make a personal explanation; he may do so.
Mr NEWMAN-Earlier in this debate, the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins) alleged that in regard to an earlier incident I had been forced by the National Country Party to come into this House to tell a lie. That incident was debated on the occasion of that allegation. It was refuted then; and 1 refute it now. It is totally false.

I note an observation in Hansard on 1 April 1980 by the honourable member for Diamond Valley, the present Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, when he said:
We have the principle of ministerial responsibility under the system of government that was elected in free and fair elections in Rhodesia. It is a pity it is not practised more often here.
We might say 'Amen' to that. In the discussion earlier this afternoon, in the defence by the Prime Minister of the Minister for Communications there was an attempt to skate over the exchange between the Senate Committee and the Minister for Communications. The impression was created that there had just been a casual misunderstanding; that there was no real difference of opinion and that it was possible for an honest mistake to be made on both sides. I point out that there were nine stages in the conflict between the evidence of Mr Tony Webster, the then Chairman of the Australian Dairy Corporation and the evidence of the Minister for Communications about his term as the Minister for Primary Industry. I just mention the nine stages briefly to make sure that the House is fully conversant with what is in the report. The first stage was the evidence on oath of Mr Webster given on 18 February 1981. The relevant part is found on page 148 of the report at which Mr Webster is reported as saying:
I reported it-
That is, the details about excess skim milk powder and the problem of violating the terms on which the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade arrangement was operating-
back to the board; I reported it to the Minister; I took it upon myself as chairman to carry the responsibility for making the decision and making that knowledge available. Something had to be done. I did it and reported what I had done. Of course it was on my mind.
He then said:
The Minister noted what I said:
At page 150 of the report there is a letter dated 27 February 1981 from Senator Peter Rae, the Chairman of the Committee, inviting the Minister to make a submission in response, either in writing or orally. The third stage is the response from the Minister to Senator Rae dated 16 March 1981. The relevant part is on page 154 of the report.

This third stage is the first major evasion. The Minister replied:
I do not recall any discussion with Mr Webster on the nature of the outcome of the negotiations with HOMPI on the rebate following his return from Manila. Certainly, no evidence was provided to me to indicate that the ADC share of the credit in the rebate account was being used to continue the rebate in breach of the GATT minimum price. Such an arrangement, if known about, would have been totally unacceptable to the Government.
In other words, there was no indication that oral evidence would be provided. The letter, of course, could be regarded as a submission. The fourth stage is a letter from Senator Peter Rae to the Minister for Communications dated 25 March 1981. It is found on page 157 of the report. It states:
There is a clear discrepancy between the sworn evidence given by Mr Webster on this matter and your recollections as expressed in your letter of 16 March. I am writing on behalf of the Committee to ask if you would be prepared to repeat your recollections in the form of a statutory declaration to give them the same evidentiary value as the statements. made by Mr Webster. The Committee considers that in the interests of justice the evidence which so clearly contradicts Mr Webster's evidence should be provided on the same formal, sworn basis.
The fifth stage is in the form of a response from the Minister and is a letter dated 22 April 1981 which is found on page 158 of the report. The Minister stated:
My response to your earlier correspondence of the 16th March, 1981, was written as a Minister in accordance with the several Oaths taken relating to that office.
That is the second evasion. The next responsethe sixth stage-is a letter from Senator Rae to the Minister for Communications dated 6 May and is found on page 159 of the report. It states:
From our examination of the oaths taken by Ministers we are unable to see any declaration that would place written evidence given by a Minister on the same basis as sworn evidence.
I am therefore instructed to repeat the request made in my letter to you dated 25 March 1981 that you reconsider placing your recollections in the form of a statutory declaration to give them the same evidentiary value as the statements made by Mr Webster.
What is the response? The response-the seventh stage-is a personal explanation on 7 May in this House by the minister. He stated:
I do not believe it appropriate that a Minister of this House should appear and give sworn evidence before a committee of the other house. Therefore, I make this statement with respect to the evidence that I have tendered to that Committee in the Parliament and, of course, in accordance with my responsibilities to this Parliament.
What is left out? What is left out is the response to the request to make a statutory declaration. At that stage the Committee had not said 'appear'. It said: 'All right, put it on oath but put it on paper'. We have a complete evasion from the Minister. He declines even to advert to the specific point that was raised in the letter by Senator Peter Rae.

1618 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

The next stage-the eighth stage-is a letter on 12 May from the Minister to Senator Peter Rae in which he simply says that he stated the facts of the matter in the house and that that should have the same weight as a statutory declaration. Again there is a reluctance to put anything on paper, to sign it and to put it on oath. That is the fourth evasion. The ninth step in this process is a statement by Senator Rae to the Senate which is found on pages 164 to 166 of the report in which he goes through the whole matter and also points out that, from the point of view of the Committee, the evidence given was not very satisfactory.
It was pointed out quite correctly in debate this afternoon that the cross-examination of Mr Webster was perhaps not as rigorous as it might have been. I think it is fair to say that if the cross examination of Mr Webster was not as rigorous, not as thorough, as it would be if it were done now, this surely is because at the time the Committee was unaware that the Minister would be telling a different story. The committee had no reason to believe at that stage that what the Chairman was saying would be the subject of contention. It had no reason to assume that the Minister was going to deny it. The Minister at that stage had not spoken about it. The other point that is raised, of course, is the suggestion that the evidence of the Minister is strongly supported by Mr Wallace, the then and current First Assistant Secretary of the Industry No. 2 (Dairy Products) Division. I think in order to dispel the idea that the evidence of Mr Wallace was of particular assistance to the Minister,-I should just quote this statement which is found on page 172 of the report. This is Senator Evans's cross-examination of Mr Wallace which reads:
Senator EVANS-Can you answer me yes or no? Were you present at any meeting between the Minister and Mr Webster, after 19 November 1976, in which the matter of the Manila agreement was discussed?
Mr Wallace-To the very best of my recollection, no.
Senator EVANS-Your recollection has not been all that bad on other matters, Mr Wallace. Can you tell me without prefacing it by that phrase, yes or no.
Mr Wallace-I would have to say no categorically because the Minister said he did not discuss it with Mr Webster.
CHAIRMAN-Mr Wallace, I think it would be fair for me, as Chairman, to say that the laughter that followed your last answer was at, not with you. I wonder whether we could try to keep this inquiry on some basis which one could expect out of a very senior public servant and not have it turned into some sort of farce.
Mr Nixon-Keep on reading.
Mr BARRY JONES-The report then went on to say:
Mr Wallace-My comments were not meant to be facetious, I can assure you of that.

CHAIRMAN. The questions are being directed to you, asking you to exercise your memory as to what happened.
Mr Nixon-Yes, keep on going.
Mr BARRY JONES-The Minister will have the opportunity to keep on reading if he wishes. He will see that what I have quoted is absolutely in context. I think the fact that there was a unanimous view in the Committee of the unreliability of Mr WalIace's evidence needs to be stressed. That is an important point.
This is a government which is notoriously unreliable so far as its word is concerned. We were assured that there would be no re-imposition of tertiary fees but the razor gang put an end to that. It is not very long since the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs was giving fulI encouragement for extending projects for the Community Youth Support Scheme and now it has been terminated. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) promised last March that there would be no increases in indirect taxes unless there were substantial reductions in income tax. Now we have massive increases in sales tax and income tax colIections that will rise by 22 per cent this year. The Prime Minister has promised lower interest rates; they are now at their highest level ever.
The method of toughing it out, which has been adopted by the Minister for Communications and also by the Minister for Industrial Relations is the only approach that the Government is trying. In the case of the Minister for Communications it has been a direct attack against members of the Committee. Senator Rae is attacked because he is known to be a Fraser opponent. Senator Withers is attacked because he is not exactly in the Fraser camp. However, the Committee was unanimous in the report. The report includes the support of one of the Prime Minister's strongest allies, Senator Austin Lewis. It is nonsense for the Minister for Communications to try to rely on his ministerial and Executive Council oaths to put his evidence on the same basis as Mr Webster. If the Minister does not want the evidence to be tested on an equal basis he should set up either a royal commission or a select committee of this House which will be able to hear his evidence on oath. As Senator Rae pointed out, either Mr Webster lied on oath or the Minister has lied in the House. Either possibility-they are the only possibilities-leads to serious consequences. If Mr Webster lied under oath then charges must be brought against him for perjury. If the Minist~r for Communications lied to the House then he IS unfit to remain as a Minister. The Government wishes to have it both ways. It is trying to tough it out in respect of the Minister but to take no action against Mr Webster. The Government tells us

that the Minister did not lie in the House. If that is the case the Government really has little other alternative but to charge Mr Webster.
This Government will do anything to try to stay in office. It proved itself to be disaster-prone. It is reeling down the corridors of power. It is a danger to itself, a threat to the democratic process and a menace to Australia. It is positively phosphorescent with corruption. It needs to be exposed. If the Government wants to bring on an early election there is nothing that would be welcomed more by the Opposition. In 1975 we heard much talk about reprehensible circumstances. A number of Ministers, then leading members of the Opposition, used every weapon they could against the then government. In the present circumstances, the least that the Government can do is to apply some consistency in the way in which it operates the Government. If Senator Withers had to go then it seems absolutely appropriate that the Deputy Leader of the National Country Party should also go. I think it is appalling. The Government should resign. I hope that the House will carry this censure motion.
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (8.20)-The previous speaker, the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones), made a number or errors during his speech, one of which was that either Mr Webster had perjured himself or the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) had misled the House. The fact is that the Government has not said that Mr Webster perjured himself. What we have said in this debate to this point is that the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations did not follow up his failure to answer questions specifically and categorically. That should be on the record. The Opposition simply launched this censure motion to try to make some political capital out of the report of that Committee on the Australian Dairy Corporation and its Asian subsidiaries. The trouble is that I doubt that the Opposition has really tried to analyse the report. It believes that abuse will beat truth and that if enough mud is thrown some of it

will stick. The fact is that the matters in this report dealing with my colleague the Minister for Communications are pretty thin. The Opposition thinks that there is mileage in it and by latching on to this aspect of the report the Opposition really is demonstrating how shallow it is.
The Committee has unequivocally supported evidence given by Mr Webster on the General Agreement on Tarriffs and Trade breach issue rather than that of Mr Sinclair, a Minister of the Crown, and two other people, Mr O'Bryan and Mr Wallace. On page 174 of its report the Committee states:
We have weighed up all the . . . evidence set out above . . . the Committee concludes that on the balance of probabilities, Mr Sinclair's recollection of the events was not as reliable as that of Mr Webster.
The Committee's main reasons for such findings are seemingly that Mr Sinclair refused to give sworn evidence and that the evidence given by Mr Wallace was guarded, qualified and evasive. On the first point, the Committee has rejected Mr Sinclair's argument that his evidence was given as a Minister in accordance with the several oaths related to his office. But it has accepted the significance of Mr Sinclair's stating his case in a personal explanation to the Parliament on 7 May. On page 168 of the report the Committee stated:
. . . he confirmed his recollections in clear and unequivocal terms in his statement to the House of Representatives. This was certainly a very serious step on his part because of the sanctions which can be imposed on a Minister, and a member, for misleading the House.
The Committee, therefore, recognised that Mr Sinclair's statement was of major significance. It even went on to spell out in some detail the sanctions which are available to the Parliament in dealing with someone who has misled the House. Having accepted the gravity and significance of what Mr Sinclair had done in making a statement to the Parliament, the Committee still chose to prefer Mr Webster's evidence because it had been made as sworn evidence before the Committee. Turning to the evidence as provided in the Committee's report, one is bound to make the observation that if, as the Committee finds, Mr Wallace's evidence was:
. . . so guarded and qualified, and his manner so evasive, that far from providing unqualified support for Mr Sinclair's versions of the events, Mr Wallace at best only offered luke-warm and tentative confirmation-
Then so too was the evidence given by Mr Webster. According to the evidence reproduced in the Committee's report, Mr Webster at no stage categorically stated that he had told Mr Sinclair that the GATT minimum was still being breacheq in spite of a request by Mr Sinclair that the Corporation should renegotiate the arrangements so as to

1620 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

observe the GATT minimum price. According to the transcript, Senator Evans asked:
What did you tell the Minister about the outcome of your Manila negotiations on November IS?
Mr Webster said:
I told the Minister the basis on which we had negotiated.
Throughout this interview with the Committee, Mr Webster answered specific questions with such responses as: 'The details were given orally'; 'I advised the Minister of the facts'; and 'I could not honestly recollect specifically what the conversation was'. Let me repeat that: 'I could not honestly recollect specifically what the conversation was'. Nowhere has Webster categorically confirmed that the Minister was told that the breach was continuing. If anything his answers were guarded, qualified and evasive. Also, it might well be asked: Why did the Committee not seek from Mr Webster during this interview two fundamental facts-when did his alleged meeting with Mr Sinclair take place and where did it take place? What sort of committee inquiry was this? Those questions seem to be fairly fundamental to the argument and yet they were not asked at the time.
The second aspect of this particular piece of evidence is the question of whether a departmental officer was present at a meeting with Mr Sinclair and Mr Webster. Again we find that Mr Webster is unable to be categoric. The transcript reads:
Senator EVANS- . . . I want to clarify whether any actual minute was taken by anyone present of the discussion with the Minister.
Mr Webster-l personally never took minutes of any of my discussions with the Minister.
Senator EVANS-Do you know if any officer of the Department of Primary Industry was present during that discussion?
Mr Webster-l think so, but I would not be categoric.
Mr Webster on two other occasions during that part of the evidence said that he could not be categoric or specific. Yet the Committee decided that a meeting had taken place, that a departmental officer was present and that Mr Sinclair was told of the continuing GATT breach. Let us see what Mr Wallace had to say on this. I noticed that the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) heaped abuse on Mr Wallace while invoking references to the Senate Committee report but did not quote from the evidence. Senator Evans asked MrWallace:
Can you answer me yes or no? Were you present at any meeting between the Minister and Mr Webster after 19 November 1976, in which the matter of the Manila agreement was discussed?
Mr Wallace said:

To the very best of my recollection, no.
There is nothing very evasive or vague about that, but it did not satisfy the Committee. Even in answer to a question from Senator Lewis, Mr Wallace replied:
I did not attend any meeting.
The honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) was also very selective in his quotations this afternoon, but we cannot deny the firmness of Mr Wallace's reply:
I did not attend any meeting.
The Committee persisted. It is almost as though it was trying to force some other answer fromMr Wallace. Looking closely at the evidence, it is hard to justify why the Committee considered Mr Wallace's evidence to be guarded and qualified and yet not Mr Webster's. Furthermore, why did the Committee choose to prefer Mr Webster's evidence on this point rather than Mr Sinclair's when, in another section of the report, the Committee finds that Mr Webster's evidence is highly suspect? In.the section dealing with the provision of a motor car to Mr Pyle, the Committee states on page 346:
The opinions quoted by Mr Webster and Mr Pyle are riddled with inaccuracies.
The question must be asked: How can the Committee see credibility in one part of Mr Webster's evidence when it crucifies it in another part? Senator Rae, speaking on PM on 17 September, said of Mr Wallace's evidence, in relation to the question of whether or not there was a meeting between Mr Sinclair and Mr Webster:
That was probably the worst piece of evidence I have ever had the misfortune to hear given.
That is far stronger than the Committee's finding that Mr Wallace's evidence 'at best only offered luke-warm and tentative confirmation' and is nowhere near as strong as the finding that Mr Webster's evidence, in relation to Mr Pyle's car, was 'riddled with inaccuracies'. Again on Natiollwide on 17 September we findSerialor Rae referring to the 'unqualified way in which Mr Webster gave evidence'. As I have shown, there is nothing unqualified about that evidence. So much for that aspect of the report.
Let me now turn to the broader issues that are contained in it. Let me stress that I believe there are some valid recommendations in the report which I will refer to later. Nonetheless, there is little new in the report that the Government has not been aware of and that the Government has not been acting on for some considerable time. Of course, it ought to be remembered that this report was initiated only because leaked sections of the Auditor-General's reports were tabled in the Senate. Fraser Government

I have given the outlines of actions that the Government has taken as a result of the Auditor General's reports. I will go into greater specific detail in the statement that I will be making to the Parliament when my Department has received the police report into the appropriateness of certain expenditure by the former chairman of AD!. Only then will I be in a proper position to make a fully detailed statement. I will table all relevant documents, as I have said on past occasions. I believe the broad outline that I have given does, however, clearly demonstrate that many of the points raised by the Senate Committee have already been rectified. It also completely disproves the statement by the Committee on page 202 of its report which, in referring to the Auditor-General's report, states:
The report was referred to the ADC by the Minister for Primary Industry for appropriate action. However, so far as the Committee is aware, The ADC has not yet completed any necessary action nor reported to the Minister on its progress.
It says: 'So far as the Committee is aware'. How could the Committee be aware when it did not ask any questions about the issues. The fact is that the Committee made no effort to find out what changes in procedures had been made. It asked a few general questions but made no effort to find out specifically what changes had been made to the relationship between the ADC and the ADI and their relationship to the Department of Primary Industry. I think the Leader of the Opposition demonstrated, in his speech, that he is too lazy or too incompetent to check. He simply attacked me and stupidly and offensively attacked officers of my Department who cannot reply. The fact is that actions have been taken. The Corporation has been consistently reporting to me. It has been working in close co-operation with me and with my Department to ensure that appropriate actions are taken to overcome the deficiencies noted by the Auditor-General.
Honourable members will recall that, acting on , legal advice, I requested my Department to report certain matters relating to the appropriateness of certain expenditure of the former chairman of ADI to the Australian Federal Police. That was done on 13 August 1980. The police provided my Department with a report on 24 October 1980. This was referred to the Crown Solicitor for advice. The Crown Solicitor advised that there were some aspects which required further police investigations. These matters were referred to the police on 21 November 1980. Since then, further investigations have been carried out. There have
been consultations between the police, my Department and the Crown Solicitors' office. I

expect that the police will be reporting to my Department in the near future.
I turn now to the attack that has been made on the credibility of the Department of Primary Industry and particularly on its senior officers. I think the Senate Committee has been unduly harsh and, indeed, totally unfair, in its findings on Mr Wallace who is a fine public servant and who has given great service to primary industry of this country. He has the respect of the farming industries. He is certainly not deserving of the grubby criticisms that have come from the Committee or from members of the Opposition, particularly in the way the Committee has supported Mr Webster's evidence and condemned Mr Wallace's. It is also clear from what I have stated, that many initiatives have already been taken in regard to the ADC and ADI of which the Committee was obviously not aware. It is a fact that Mr Wallace has devoted a great deal of his time over the past three years to this subject. It has been of tremendous concern to him, the Secretary of my Department and to me as Minister.
The Opposition has now found it opportune to try to link so-called bureaucratic inefficiencies over Asia Dairy Industries with the problems we faced over the recent meat substitution issue. Of course, it is easy to accuse when sitting on the Opposition benches. But the truth is that the facts do not substantiate the claims. In fact, as the honourable member for Calare (Mr MacKenzie) pointed out last week, within 19 days of Australia being advised of the original substantiated notification of horsemeat substitution, the issue had been wrapped up. We had instigated a top priority police investigation and charges have already been made against one man. We have secured the release of the bulk of Australian meat that was being withheld from the market in the United States. We set up a royal commission into the meat industry. Our meat export trade to the United States is back to a normal footing. All of our actions have been taken in close co-operation with the United States authorities. They have expressed satisfaction with the way we have moved properly, promptly and decisively on the issue.
The real test of our actions is reflected in the price in the United States for Australian beef. It is fact that it is now dearer than it was prior to the discovery of horsemeat. Given all the actions that we have taken on this issue in a very short space of time, for anyone toclaim bureaucratic inefficiency is just plain stupidity and shows a gross ignorance of the facts. The whole approach taken by the Opposition on the meat issue, as with the Senate Committee report, is purely political. I

believe that it is the Opposition that should be censured.
