Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (8.42)-We have just heard another tedious speech from another senior Minister, this time the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon). We have heard a few of them in this deba.te. The speeches, which were written over the weekend and read in this Parliament, do not apply themselves to the arguments of this debate. They simply seek to save the collective skins of the Ministers of this discredited Government. Like other written speeches, including that of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), the speech of the Minister for Primary Industry not only did not properly apply itself to

the Opposition's censure motion but also was a waste of time for this Parliament. There was a lit-
any of what the Auditor-General reported and what is now, belatedly, being done. We on this side of the Parliament, and I believe the majority of people in this country, are now interested not so much in what is being done after the horse has bolted as in what will be done to improve the administration of ministries such as the one of the Minister for Primary Industry to make sure that these sorts of incompetent blunders do not occur in future.
The essence of the Opposition's censure motion is the failure to ensure proper and adequate standards by senior Ministers, such as the Minister for Primary Industry, before the problem is upon us. It is their duty to see that the administration of their departments prevents these things from happening. We do not want a whole day of this Parliament to be taken up by Ministers coming here with speeches prepared hours ago in their offices by someone and trying to whitewash the situation. We want to hear what they will do about improving their administration. The case put forward today by the Opposition has been overwhelmingly that their administration needs vastly greater attention than it has been getting.-
So far we have concentrated on the performance of the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) in the role of Minister for Primary Industry and of the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner). But we may well have focused our attention on the Minister for Primary Industry to a far greater extent. There ought to be other opportunities in this Parliament for us to do just that, as was promised last week. As far as I know the report of the Senate Standing Committee on Finance and Government Operations has not yet been tabled in this Parliament. We expect to get another opportunity to debate the shocking events that have unfolded in that report and to get a further whitewashing effort from the Minister as he seeks to explain away something that is inexplicable.
In passing, let us look at the Minister's administration. He administers the Australian Wheat Board. There have been scandals there. He administers the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation. At least he had the decency to remind us in the course of his remarks of last week's scandal there. Now we have the scandal about the Australian Dairy Corporation. I repeat, we will hold our fire on him. Other opportunities will be available to us and I trust that they will be available to us very soon. Most importantly, to get the censure motion into perspective, it is vital to recognise the sinister role of the Prime Minister in the lousy

1624 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Fraser Government

administration of this country. I will return to the details of that a little later. To set the scene, let me suggest to the House four valid grounds for a censure motion of this sort. There may be many others, but I will suggest just four. The first one is incompetence. If ever we have seen a story of incompetence unfolded we have seen it today in the case put forward by Opposition speakers in this censure debate. We have seen it in relation to the performance of the Minister for Industrial Relations and of the Minister for Primary Industry. I will come back later to those details.
The second valid reason for a censure motion is the misleading of this Parliament. We do not want people who peddle untruths to govern us. We have standards and traditions to maintain in this Parliament and we have conventions to help us to maintain those standards and traditions. I believe the vast majority of the people in this country will
agree with the assertion that those standards have been sorely tried by the Administration that this country of ours is suffering under at the present time. A third criterion for a censure motion is major blunders of the Administration. Could there be any questioning of this as a ground for censuring a government? We are all about good administration in a parliament and seeking to improve the lives of those we represent. Poor administration and the sorts of blunders that have been highlighted today will not achieve anything in the way of improving the standard of living of those we represent. The fourth criterion I want to draw to the attention of this House is, of course, another valid ground for a censure motion. It is the plotting to cover up the blunders. If we ever have had evidence of plotting to cover up blunders, we have it in what has unfolded today in this debate. This smacks legitimately of sinister plots and conspiracies to cover up the truth. It is an enemy of good government. We should not allow any Administration to get away with this sort of thing.
I will now examine in greater detail the evidence that has been exposed today in the light of those standards which I have laid down as being proper criteria to judge whether a government should be censured. I will start with the actions of the present-I hope temporary-Minister for Industrial Relations. The incontrovertible facts of this miserable episode that has unfolded around him are as follows: He told a House of Representatives Estimates committee within the last fortnight that when he took over the Ministry in April he was not pleased with the progress that had been made by his predecessor in relation to the deregistration of the Builders Labourers Federation. That case was put forward at the meeting of

that committee by the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock) and the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke). In short, they mauled him at the committee meeting and he came away so chagrined that he wanted to do something to try to re-establish his reputation. Consequently, he set up a question without notice which, incidentally abused the forms of the House.
The question was put by the honourable member for Kingston (Mr Chapman) on Tuesday 15 September. The express purpose of the question was to enable an answer to be given denigrating the honourable member for Kooyong. The fight for leadership in the Liberal Party takes its toll. I think it is a proper role of an Opposition to seek, in the best way possible, to minimise the toll for the ordinary people in this country and to try to bring matters to a head so that we can get some resolution of the fight quickly and get on with good government. The happiest resolution would be to call an election. We would very much challenge the Government to do just this following one scandal after another. The gist of the answer to the honourable member for Kingston by the Minister for Industrial Relations was:
. . . I think it is appropriate that J place some facts on the record so as to show the circumstances surrounding preparation of the case and the state of unpreparedness when I assumed responsibility for the portfolio.
If ever there was a charge of incompetence on his predecessor, it was in those words. The state of unpreparedness is nothing more nor less than an attack on his Liberal colleague and part of that faction fight that I have already made reference to. Let me place on record that I hold no brief for the honourable member for Kooyong. He is a personable fellow, but I believe he is a political lightweight trying to be all things to all men. I have said it and I mean just that. The sooner he gets the job and the people learn what the pros know, the better. Having said that, I do not condone in any way the actions of the Minister for Industrial Relations. These faction fights lead to bad government. I repeat that it is the job of the Opposition to try to do something about bringing this to a head so we have some resolution and can get on with the good administration of this country.
So far the faction fight in this context has led to two blunders. We have the document which has been incorporated in Hansard. It has done enormous damage to Costain Australia Ltd, the company named in it. It was a shocking blunder and, indeed, it has cost not only that company dearly but also the building trade enormously. That is just one fall-out from this episode in this faction fight. But there is another blunder. Earlier

in this debate today we learned from the honourable member for Wills that the unpreparedness charge was just not true. I am sure it would escape any objective observer of this situation in this House today. That information was damaging not only to Costainbut also, as I said earlier, to the whole building industry. I believe that for this reason alone the Minister should resign or should be sacked.
The other criterion is the cover-up. Like the Prime Minister's cover-up, it was based on seeking to achieve defence by the best form of attack. It was stated that because the Builders Labourers Federation was advantaged by the blunders, somehow this was the fault of the Opposition. It is impossible to go through the evidence given by Mr Webster and then to say of him that he was an unequivocal witness, reliable witness, a substantial witness, or a believable witness; one could use what adjectives one liked. All the way through his evidence he was saying: 'I am not too sure'; 'I cannot be categoric about this'. But what really lines up against Mr Webster in terms of accepting his credibility is simply this. A memorandum m writing was sent to Mr Webster by Mr Norwood relating to the purchase--
Mr Scott-A letter in writing?

Fraser Government

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1629

Mr KILLEN-I am sorry, I accept the solecism. Yes, it was a memorandum. In the case of the honourable member who has just interjected we would need to carve it in granite. We could start with his magnificent head. This case involved the purchase of a car which would have represented a grave breach of government rules. But Mr Webster said: 'I have no recollection of the memorandum'. How strange it is that the principal witness, accepted by the Committee when it refers to the conduct of business orally, has a splendid reputation but as far as the memorandum is concerned the Committee found that he said: '1 have no recollection of it'. The Committee report went on to state that Mr Webster's evidence was riddled with inaccuracy. I ask my honourable friends opposite: Would they like any tribunal to accept that and, on that basis, to make a finding which affects their reputation, their honour and their character? I doubt it very much. If they take a contrary point of view, 1 would be very sad indeed.

Finally, I say this about committees that proc ceed in this quasi judicial fashion: 1 think the Parliament owes it to its own integrity and to the
nation to ensure that these committees are conducted in a fashion in which the integrity and the reputation of people can be and is protected. I have made this suggestion before. 1 have slender respect for committees that proceed in this fashion. 1 have no admiration for those who walk in the tradition of the star chamber. I believe that those people who want to make a case can make it with all the vigour in the world, but certain prin-
ciples are to be observed. I believe the Parliament would serve itself and future generations well by ensuring that precisely that was done.

COMPANIES AND SECURITIES LEGISLATION (MISCELLANEOUS
AMENDMENTS) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 10 September, on motion by Mr Moore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr JOHN BROWN (Parramatta) (9.44) - The legislation which we are about to debate is entitled the Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Bill 1981. As this' deals with the very important matter of the regulation and, perhaps, clarification of company law in Australia it seems quite disturbing that the Government has not listed one honourable member to speak on this matter. Unless a couple jump out of the ground in the near future it seems that the Government is not prepared to offer any sort of debate in the area of the reform of company law. I do not suppose that is all that surprising in view of the fact that the Government has made no effort in the past and it does not appear to want to make much effort to see that corporate crime in Australia is removed. In fact, today we have probably seen a fairly good example in this House of corporate criminals being defended.
The legislation which we are now debating amends in a large number of relatively minor ways the various codes of the co-operative companies and securities scheme. The Opposition does not oppose any of the clauses of the Bill. We, however, continue to regard the co-operative scheme as deficient in a great number of respects. Accordingly I move:

1632 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981 Companies and Securities Legislation Bill

That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing this bill, the House-
(I) expresses its concern-
(a) at the Government's abdication of its responsibility to make national laws for companies and securities pursuant to the Commonwealth's own constitutional powers, and in accordance with the recommendations of the Senate Select Committee on Securities and Exchange in 1974 (the Rae Report);
(b) at the lack of effective accountability of the National Companies and Securities Commission to either the Government or Parliament of the Commonwealth, or to any other single elected Government or Parliament; and
(c) that the Parliament of the Commonwealth is not given an opportunity to effectively scrutinise Bills introduced under the Cooperative Companies and Securities Scheme and will not be able effectively to determine company law in the future under this scheme;
(2) calls upon the Government in the event of the breakdown for any reason of the scheme, of which this Bill is a part, immediately to proceed to the introduction of Commonwealth legislation for the national regulation of companies;
(3) calls upon the Government to expedite the establishment of an Accounting Standards Review Board, and
(4) calls upon the Government immediately to pursue a fundamental reform of company law and, as the first stage in such reform, to institute the Companies and Securities Law Revision Committee without delay'.
That amendment will be seconded by the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi). Before making some comments about the matter raised in the amendment to the motion for the second reading I mention where this amendingBill takes us. It was found that it will be necessary for two main reasons: Firstly, the process of drafting with the need for approval from seven governments is so tedious and inefficient that many corrections and clarifications are needed even before the companies code has come into effect; and secondly, a number of improvements are found necessary following criticisms from the Opposition, particularly from the honourable member for Hawker, who has contributed so much to debates on companies and securities matters. The Opposition welcomes these improvements in relation, for example, to professional legal privilege for the definition of officers in certain clauses. We wish only that the Government were as responsible and responsive on matters of principle raised by the Opposition as it has proved to be on matters of detail. That is a continuing criticism that we made of the Government in the various debates that we have had in this House. Unfortunately, these criticisms seem to fall on deaf ears and unresponsive and perhaps unthinking heads.

It is the fundamental nature of the companies and securities schemes which is most at fault in this situation. We have essentially national markets for securities in Australia. I am sure that no one will seriously argue that, for instance, Industrial Equity Ltd, controlled by Mr Ron Brierley, or Endeavour Resources Ltd, an affiliate of Bond Corporation Holdings Ltd, operate only in New South Wales and Victoria. Yet we have in the case of each of these companies, which have engaged in takeover bids which may be in breach of the ac~ quisition of shares code or other codes, the nonsensical situation of a Federal body using State agencies to investigate activities conducted nationally under the auspices of the. laws which effectively reside within State jurisdictions.
There can be no doubt that these takeovers represent a major test of the National Companies and Securities Commission because it is clear .that the corporate operators who have work~d virtually free of regulation in this country throughout the era of renewed share market interest, which began in about 1977, have none of the respeyt for the Commission that similar agencies command in the United States of America or the United Kingdom. The National Companies and Securities Commission, after years of delay and procrastination by this Government under the guise of the new federalism policy-that awful term-has no reserve of trust and respect. It will have to test its arm in Australia's courts which are notorious for their literal interpretation of the law. Moreover, under a co-operative scheme we must remember that different courts will have jurisdiction in different States. Who knows what manner of rival and inconsistent interpretation will appear over time when the different cases are dealt with?
I mention the situation in these Cases in respect of the takeover law because I believe that they deserve the attention of this Parliament. They certainly deserve the attention of the Government. As I said at the outset, they deserve some members of the Government taking sufficient interest in this very important area at least to participate in the debate. I am very concerned that the National Companies and Securities Commission has been put in this situation as a result of the way in which the co-operative scheme has evolved and been implemented. We in the Opposition believe that the Parliament of the Commonwealth should have the exclusive responsibility for this body of law. We do not have that situation now. Rather, we have seven parliaments, which in effect means none. It is very difficult to establish real responsibility in a parliament when six others can .always be blamed. In this respect, I note with concern the second reading speech of the Minister

Companies and Securities Legislation Bill 22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1633

for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore). He said:
At the last meeting of the Ministerial Council, it was agreed that during 1982 there would be no substantive amendments to the initial legislation.
Mr Jacobi-A moratorium.
Mr JOHN BROWN-As the honourable member for Hawker says, it is a moratorium on law breakers. The reasons givenfor this are, firstly, that it gives time for everybody to get used to things and, secondly, that it gives time to look at hew reform proposals. How very cosy. Let us all relax. Let us all sink into a torpor of corporate insensibility. What this superficially reasonable proposition fails to mention is that we have been reviewing company law in Australia at the national level since 1959. I think Sir Garfield Barwick probably left this Parliament in frustration at not being able to get any of that legislation which he tried to implement off the deck. Then we had the sad situation between 1972 and 1975 of Mr Enderby from the Labor Party finding the same frustration. Whatever reform he tried to introduce was frustrated in the Senate.
We have had numerous inquiries and reports by the Law Reform Commission, by Senate committees, by the Eggleston Committee, by the R~e Committee and so on over two decades. The legIslative codes to which the Minister referred, at least in the case of company law are little changed from the Interstate Corporate Affairs Commission versions and the takeover code has been waiting for government action for several. ye~rs. All this past pressure for reforms and all thiS pnor experience and awareness of the bodies of.law ~o which we refer. hardly need a settlmg m period-in effect of about two years. The Minister has signalled to the takeover merchants and the manipulators: 'Hang in there and find loopholes because once you find them you have two years to play with them'. Some of the very smart manipulators in Australia are playing with those loopholes very effectively right now. This puts extraordinary pressure on the NCSC which does not even know which jurisdiction to turn to for help. I venture to suggest that if a problem of a similar nature arises in Queensland the Commission might as well forget about it and not bother to get involved because the record of abdication of responsibility by the Queensland Government gives no cause for joy here in-Canberra. How extraordinary it is that no parliament can really be held to account for failures and weaknesses in this body of law which is so vital to the development of the Australian economy, the assembly of capital resources and the management of our corporate enterprises.

We in the Labor Party want the national Parliament to undertake this role, and that is why I have moved the amendment on behalf of the Opposition. We are hoping for the e.stablishment .o~ a standing committee on compames and secuntles to look at this law continually and to consider the much needed reforms. At the same time, I have reiterated the Opposition's call for an accounting standards review board to ensure that the vital units measures and principles of accounting are give; statutory force. Prese.ntly there is no. real body of law which covers thiS and we ha~e dlff~r ing accounting standards all over AustralIa which make problems for people of inexpert ability who try to read company reports or financial statements. It all leads to a weakness in the system which, of course, eventually leads to the smart manipulators being able to operate outside whatever law there is.
The Opposition has moved an amendment in respect of the entire scheme. We set out many more when the Companies Bill proceeded through the Parliament in the last ~ession, but obviously the Government saw fit to Ignore many of these if the amendments in the present Bill are representative of its thinking. I would like to mentionone of these in particular. In that debate we moved for a reform of company law that required that. companies disclose all donations for political purposes. This issue has .been discu.ssed by the Ministerial Council, but In the environment of minimum change it was deferred. Mr Deputy Speaker, I think you would probably have noted, even from the cosy atmosphere in Queensland on Saturday, that the people of New South Wal.es have just voted overwhelmingly in favour of diSclosure by politicians of their pecuniary interests. This is one measure of the attitude of people to disclosure in the field of politics. I hope that the Government will take some sort of message from that vote because it seems to us very odd that large donations made to political pa~ties in Australia are now not a matter for publIc scrutiny. The new legislation in New South Wal~s allows public funding of elections. Of course, It has been ignored by the Liberal Party. I dare say that in the next week or two the Liberal Party will be bemoaning and bewailing the fact that it did not take advantage of the legislation when it misses out on the couple of million dollars it might have got.
That legislation points out the weakness in this particular case-there is .no proper. ~eason w~y disclosure of large donatIOns to polItICal parties should not be made. Major corporations are formed principally in the interests of their shareholders, and the shareholders have a right to know

1634 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981 Companies and Securities Legislation Bill

where their money is going. Creditors a,lso have a right to know. If I were a shareholder of some of the major companies which I know and which you, Mr Deputy Speaker, know contribute very heavily to the Government parties, I would like to know that my money was going to such an inefficient and, dare I say, dishonest Government. The Minister sent to me a report on the Opposition's view on 5 June this year. Of this proposal he said:
. . . it can be argued that this issue should be determined outside the parameters of company law and applied to companies only to the same extent as it applies to individuals.
I refute that view. It reveals a failure to comprehend what company law and the rules of disclosure are all about. The boards of companies, unlike individuals, are not using their own money. They have access to vast sums of other people's money and they are protected by the corporate form from full liability for the actions of the company. Responsibility and disclosure are what company law is all about. I hope that the comments made by the Department in this case are not indicative of a loss of direction with company law. I hope that the Minister will take the need for law reform more seriously in this and in the other areas which have been raised by the Opposition in various other debates and which will be raised, particularly by the honourable member for Hawker, in the course of this debate.
In the last few months there has been little done to inspire one with confidence that the new companies and securities legislation will stand the test of time. I noted that the Chanticleer column in the Australian Financial Review of 9 September stated:
The Endeavour-Northern Mining takeover is in danger of symbolising the current state of corporate law in Australiacomprehensively confused.
I think that is a rather profound statement of the exact situation with regard to company law in Australia. The practitioners in the area do not quite understand it, let alone the laymen of Australia who are investing their few hard earned dollars in public companies. There is no doubt that company law is comprehensively confused. After a few short months of operation by the NCSC it has become increasingly obvious that this assessment of the law-that it is comprehensively confused-is devastingly accurate. Throughout both the Endeavour-Northern Mining and the IEL-Huttons sagas, neither of which is yet fully played out, it became clear that the credibility and the effectiveness of both the National Companies and Securities Commission and the new takeovers code would be put to the test very stringently by the smart operators who

are now moving in with these sleazy takeover bids

covered by the corporate veil that the Opposition

seeks to have lifted in the area of company law

but which the Government is very tardy to do

anything about.

.

It is perhaps riot inappropriate that I spend just a little time examining the course of those takeovers to see just where we are not at in this
area of corporate law. I note in passing that the area of corporate law relating to takeovers covered by the monopolisation and mergers provisions of the Trade Practices Act is itself deficient and in need of overhaul. Indeed, during the Endeavour-Northern Mining battle-Mr Bond was seeking to make use of a loophole in the Trade Practices Act to gain control of the Swan brewery.

Debate interrupted.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! It being 10 p.m., I propose the question:

Mr ARMITAGE (Chifiey) (10.0)-1 draw the attention of honourable members tonight to the most disgraceful use of the forms of Parliament by the Government last week when it kept up a barrage of Dorothy Dix questions on the New South Wales elections believing as it quite obviously did that this would have a tremendous impact upon that election, resulting in the return of a Liberal National Country Party Government led by the hapless Mr McDonald. But, of course, that was not to be. Instead, the Government, led by Neville Wran, has been returned with a record majority for any Government in the history of New South Wales. We stated throughout the asking of those questions by interjection, time and time again, that it's got to be Wran. As it happened, it had to beWran.
Mr John Brown-It was Wran.
Mr ARMITAGE-I agree with the honourable member for Parramatta that it was Wran: Of course, that was, undoubtedly, a disgraceful use of the forms of the Parliament. Let us face reality. The Government, by doing this, hitched its own

Adjournment

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1635

band-wagon to that of Mr McDonald, the Leader of the Opposition in New South Wales and to the band-wagon of the Liberal and National Country parties.
Mr Les McMahon-How is Mr McDonald going?
Mr ARMITAGE-The honourable member for Sydney asked me how Mr McDonald is going. The tips are on. The bookmakers are out. They reckon it is six to one against him winning his seat. Those are the latest tips we get from the bookies in New South Wales. The facts are, as I said, that there is an extraordinary situation where this Government has hitched itself to this bandwagon. It~ therefore, has to account for and accept a very great deal of responsibility for the defeat. It shows simply that it has lost its credibility for the people of New South Wales and, for that matter, for the people of Australia. In order words, the Government stands condemned by its own actions throughout last week and the week before in supporting Mr McDonald and the Liberal and National Country parties in New South Wales. This Government can expect similar results when it finally faces up to the fact that it will be dragged to the polls in 1983.
I am greatly perturbed about information which I have received. I have heard that, in order for Mr McDonald to follow in the footsteps of the honourable member for Wentworth (Mr Coleman), there are now moves within the Liberal party hierarchy to find a seat in Canberra for Mr McDonald. Whatever happens, I do not want to see this Parliament become a graveyard for defeated leaders of the Opposition in New South Wales. After all, the honourable member for Wentworth has not exactly distinguished himself in this Parliament. But when he is compared with the aspirant for a north shore seat in this Parliament the honourable member is a world beater. It looks as though we will have to cop Mr McDonald here in Canberra. I have been making a few subtle inquiries-I am always very subtleabout which seat the Federal Liberal Party is looking at for Mr McDonald. I have heard two propositions put up. The first proposition is, of course, that the honourable member for Bradfield (Mr Connolly) will retire and hand his seat over to Mr McDonald. The honourable member for Bradfield will get a diplomatic appointment having come from the Department of Foreign Affairs originally. The other proposition is even more intriguing when one looks at the list of speakers who spoke here today. Remembering the connotation between the Federal seat of North Sydney and the State seat of North Shore, I heard that the honourable member for North Sydney

(Mr Spender) will resign and be given a judgeship. He will become a judge appointed by the Federal Government.
Mr Charles Jones-Not another Liberal judge.
Mr ARMITAGE-Yes, another Liberal appointment. All this is to be done simply to find a little spot for the poor hapless Mr McDonald who is about to go into oblivion. I think this is a disgraceful state of affairs. It would be natural for the honourable member for North Sydney to get the job. He is a boring speaker in this Parliament. He might look better on the bench; one never knows. Accordingly, we are very worried about this. We do not want this to happen. Mr GOODLUCK (Franklin) (10.5)-It is good to see the Minister for the Capital Territory, the honourable member for Denison (Mr Hodgman), sitting at the table because I wish to talk briefly about the apple industry. The Minister will be aware of an article that appeared in the Hobart Mercury on Saturday night about the decline in the apple industry in Tasmania. I have checked that report. I fj.nd that some of the facts are not there. In fact, the apples that left Tasmania last year were of the best quality and were shipped in the correct way. The marketing authority-the Minister and I have been critical of that marketing authority in Tasmania-has done a very good job. But I am afraid that the decline was caused by additional apples being placed on the United Kingdom market. Also the re-evaluation has had an effect on that industry. There has been a shortfall, but not to the extent of $ 10m that was mentioned in that article. I believe that there has been a shortfall. I will have to have a careful look at that. I am sure that the Minister for the Capital Territory will support me in a submission that I may make to Cabinet to try to get the additional assistance for the apple industry in Tasmania. He and I know that it is an extremely important industry.

Adjournment

now taken a step which, I believe, is in the right direction by making liquefied petroleum gas available beside the petrol pumps. I think, if honourable members remember, I said several years ago that, in certain European countries, LPG can be purchased from beside the petrol pumps. It is a vi-
able alternative to petrol. Cars will, can and do run economically and efficiently on LPG. But there is a sort of stigma attached to it in Australia. It has not been given the right publicity. I know that the Nissan Motor Co. (Aus) Pty Ltd, the maker of Datsun, manufactured a car to run specifically on LPG. But it did not get a market response because it was not supported. Unfortunately, it has been taken off the manufacturing list and we find that there is not a new car running specifically on LPG.

Taxi owners and fleet owners are running cars on LPG in Australia at the moment. It is a viable alternative to petrol. It is roughly about 50 per cent of the price of super petrol. When one transfers to LPG in the normal way, one finds that after about 20,000 kilometres one can recoup the costs. Let us hope that the oil companies will heed the warnings about shortages of petrol and look at the market very carefully, develop LPG and make it available beside petrol pumps in Australia.

Mr BARRY JONES-If the honourable member is prepared to withdraw, I will. I draw the attention of the House to an answer provided today by the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) to a question which I placed on notice on 26 August. I asked the question after seeing an Australian documentary, Backs to the Blast about the development of the uranium industry in South Australia. I will read the questions and answers together. Firstly, I asked:
Was radioactive ballast, taken from uranium mines in South Australia, used on the up-graded Transcontinental railway; if so, what quantity;
The answer to that question was:
Yes, 254,000 cubic yards of tailings were used from the Radium Hill Mine heavy media section of the concentration plant.
Secondly, I asked:
What type ofradioactive rocks were used?
The answer reads:
The types of rocks used were non-mineralised rock (country rock) plus small quantities of Davidite (IronTitanium Mineral with small amounts of Uranium).
Thirdly, I asked:
When were these rocks laid as ballast;
The answer reads:
The rocks were laid as ballast between 1964 and 1967.
Fourthly, I asked:
Who were the contractors who laid the ballast;
The answer reads:
The ballast was laid by South Australian Railways Day Labour Force.
The fifth part of the question I asked reads:
How much was paid for it.
The answer reads:

Adjournment

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1637

The cost and delivery of the ballast totalled $296,114.
The sixth part of the question reads:
Between what locations on the line was the radioactive ballast used;
The answer reads:
The ballast was used between Winnininnie and Cockburn.
The seventh part of the question reads:
What is the current level of. radioactivity in the relevant area;
The answer reads:
Tests conducted by the Health Commission of South Australia show that results are typical of a background for a granite area which does not constitute a health hazard.
The eighth part of the question reads:
How often is the level of radioactivity monitored:
Compare that with the previous answer. The answer is:
The level of radioactivity over this section is not monitored.
The final part of the question reads:
When was it last monitored.
The answer reads:
The tests conducted by the Health Commission were carried out early in 1981.
There is a complete inconsistency between the answers given to the seventh, eighth and ninth parts of the question. The question that needs to be taken up now is: What happened to the people who handled the ballast? It is one thing for the Australian National Railways to speed over the rail track at a comparatively high speed but it is a quite different thing if we think of what happened to the people whose job it was day in and day out actually to handle and lay the ballast. The explosion of the nine atomic tests in Australia in the 1950s was conducted in a period of total ignorance about the impact of fallout. There was no real understanding about safety precautions or about how necessary it was to protect people from' genetic and other physical damage. I ask the House to consider whether it ought to find out what happened to the workers who were involved in work on the railway. What has been the mortality rate? How does it compare with the mortality rate for other people in the community? What is the incidence of cancer in that group compared with that for random samples taken in the community at large? I think this is a very serious matter. It is scandalous that action was not taken years ago to replace the radioactive ballast or to provide some means of protection for the people who worked with it. I think that Backs to the Blast is an excellent film which all honourable

members ought to see. It provides a valuable insight into our very naivety over the whole question of radioactivity in what seems like generations ago, but in fact dates from the 1950s. One very valuable effect of the film has been to reveal that these radioactive tailings were used on the railway line. It is a matter that ought to arouse some concern in the community at large about our extreme carelessness with products that are potentially very dangerous. 1 would like to see much more information provided to the House about monitoring to ensure that no future action is taken which could prejudice the health of people who are working with such dangerous materials.

The consequence of this decision is that there is no airline flight from Sydney to Canberra between 11.35 a.m. and about 4 o'clock in the afternoon. I understand that has resulted from a decision taken by the airlines following a 33 per cent fall in passenger loadings after the large rise in air fares.
If the airlines found it uneconomic to send two aircraft off within five minutes of each other, it seems a curious decision to decide then that it is not economic for even one aircraft to go at this time. It seems to me that this is another instance of the pursuit of a two-airline policy, which in general 1 totally support, leading to a situation which is, frankly, intolerable. DEPARTMENT OF TRANSPORT
Australia News Release
81/1634 5-6-1981 No Decision Yet on A300 Cu rfew Dispensation
(Statement by the Hon. Ralph J. Hunt, M.P., Minister for Transport)
No decision had yet been made on whether or not operations of the A300 'Airbus' aircraft would be permitted during curfew hours, the Minister for Transport, Mr Ralph Hunt, said today.
'While 1 recognise that the A300 is one of the quietest airline aircraft currently available, reflecting the most up-to-date noise reduction technology, 1 am conscious of the noise sensitivity of the communities around Australian airports,' Mr Hunt said.
'I intend to take the views of those communities into account. With the introduction of the Airbus in July, communities will soon have the opportunity to fully appreciate the characteristics of the Airbus in operation,' he said.
'Discussions will also be held with the airlines and with the Aviation Industry Advisory Council.'

At present, curfews operate between the hours of II p.m. and 6 a.m. (local time) at Sydney, Brisbane, Adelaide, Essendon and Avalon Airports.

Mr Hunt said that he would not contemplate changing curfew arrangements, particularly for the A300, until all issues
had been taken into account.

Canberra

5 June 1981

Requestsfor Detailed Information

REPLIES TO REQUESTS FOR DETAILED INFORMATION
Department of the Parliamentary Library On 1 April 1981, Dr Blewett asked Mr Speaker:
(I) What formal mechanisms exist for consultation with staff in all sections of the Parliamentary Library with regard to decisions on the Library's organisation and function.
(2) What is the rate of staff turnover within the Library, by section, for each of the last 7 years.
(3) Have Library staff made any representations regarding increased staff consultation during that period; if so, when was it made and what action followed these representations.
(4) Have there been any expressions by staff about any matter which would indicate reduced staff morale during that period.

Mr Speaker-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Formal consultation procedures within the Department of the Parliamentary Library are detailed in Administrative Circular No. 1979/7 of 16 November 1979. The Principal Officers (now Head Librarian, Head Research Specialist, Head Systems and Co-ordination Unit and Senior Executive Officer) meet with the Permanent Head on a regular fortnightly basis and more frequently if required. Each Principal Officer meets with Group Directors or Section Heads, who in turn hold Group or section staff meetings at least once a month, on non-sitting days. Records of these meetings are kept and placed on departmental files.
These procedures are still in operation.
(2) It is not practicable nor meaningful because of organisational changes to calculate staff turnover rates by section for each of the past seven years. For the Department, commencements and cessations as a proportion of full-time staff at 31 December have been:

Commencements Cessations

1974 1975 1976 1977 1978 1979 1980 42.1 35.0 31.7 37.2 50.4 34.3 45.0 31.6 25.0 30.8 29.8 39.8 32.8 43.5

(3) and (4) Some officers of the Department have made many representations to various persons and organisations in recent years. At a Library Committee meeting of 27 Match 1980, a letter from the Professional Officers' Association concerning Library accommodation and staff consultation was distributed to Committee members.
The formal mechanisms (see (I) above) and the distinction between consultation and proper decision making processes were outlined and discussed by the Committee.
Department of the Parliamentary Library
On 5 June 1981, Mrs Child asked Mr Speaker:
(I) How many positions of Special Duties, Research Officer, referred to in Appendix I of the Annual Report of the Department of the Parliamentary Library for 1980 existed as at (a) I July in each year since 1974 and (b) I June 1981.

(2) What are the levels of each position.
(3) Who occupied each position as at (a) I July in each year since 1974and (b) I June 1981.

Aluminium Production
(Question No. 2308)
Mr Hurford asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Does Australia enjoy a comparative, if not absolute, cost advantage over Great Britain and the United States of America in aluminium production.
(2) Has Australia's domestic price for aluminium been significantly higher than the spot price in the United Kingdom, for example, was it 36 per cent higher than in the United Kingdom on 13 May 1981.
(3) Has the price of aluminium in Australia also been significantly higher than in the United States.
(4) Did he suggest in answer to a question without notice from the Member for Batman on 13 May 1981 (Hansard, page 2297)' that users of aluminium should, if they had any complaints about price-fixing or the alleged operations of a cartel, take the matter to the Trade Practices Tribunal.
(5) Is the Government a significant user of aluminium.
(6) Will he refer allegations of price-fixing to the Trade Practices Commission or take action in the Federal Court; if not, why not.
(7) IF the Government itself does not wish to take action will it ensure that the Trade Practices Commission has adequate resources to pursue the matter.
(8) As an alternative to referring this matter to the Commission will he help facilitate the provision of corporate legal aid to any local user of aluminium products who is aggrieved at the present pricing practices of aluminium producers but is unable to afford the cost of a private legal action in the Federal Court.
Mr Moore: The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am informed that the substantial level of actual or proposed investment in new aluminium production facilities in Australia suggests that some locations in Australia currently enjoy a cost advantage for new smelters over alternative locations in the United Kingdom and the United States.
Conversely, there are plans to construct new smelters in the United States which indicate that some locations in that country may have a cost advantage over alternative Australian locations. The relative competitiveness of existing plants in Australia and overseas is not known. Much of the existing 5 million tonnes of aluminium production capacity in

the United States is in smelters which are substantially depreciated and which benefit from low cost hydro-electric power. Accordingly their operating costs may be less than those of existing Australian smelters.
(2) There are two broad categories of prices for aluminium-producer prices representing relatively stable longer term considerations, and spot prices which relate to day-to-day situations and are comparatively volatile. Therefore direct comparison between the two prices is not always valid. This can be seen by looking at movements in the two price series over the last few years. I am advised that, in 1979-80 the London Metals Exchange (LME) spot price was for a period 40 to 50 per cent above the Australian producer price, whereas a subsequent easing of world demand resulted in the LME price falling sharply, so that in May 1981 the Australian producer price was about 36 per cent higher than the LMEprice.
(3) I am advised that producer prices in the United States have been about $A80 to $A 160 per tonne lower than Australian producer prices since early 1980. For three years or SO before then, producer prices in the United States were higher than in Australia. The range of published producer prices in the United States and the producer price in Australia have remained relatively unchanged since early 1980, but exchange rate relativities between the $US and $A have changed causing a comparative change in the prices in the two countries.
(4) Yes. However, my answer was meant to refer to the Trade Practices Commission rather than the Trade Practices Tribunal.
(5) A search of contracts arranged by the Department of Administrative Services for aluminium from I July 1978 reveals the following:

Year
1978-79 1979-80 1980-81 (t021/5/81)

Contracts arranged
65 26 37

Amount
$ 1,088,744
532,580 813,885

The above information only relates to materials procured by the Department of Administrative Services at the request of client departments. The information does not include purchases that could be made directly by departments or items that could include components of aluminium in a finished product, for example supply of buses, caravans, et cetera.
(6) I will refer to the Trade Practices Commassion, as the appropriate body to investigate such matters, any allegations made to me of price fixing of aluminium.
(7) The Government considers that adequate resources are provided to the Trade Practices Commission to undertake its statutory responsibilities. It is for the Commission to determine its own priorities in making the most efficient use of its resources.
(8) The provision of financial assistance to a person or corporate body involved in certain proceedings brought under the Trade Practices Act 1974 is a matter to be determined by the Attorney-General under section 170 of that Act. Under section 170, the Attorney-General is required to consider the criteria of 'hardship' and 'reasonableness'. Guidelines setting out the matters that the Attorney-General takes into account when considering the criteria were tabled in the Parliament on 12 March 1981.

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1647

British Pension: Effect of Exchange Rate

(Question No. 2398)

Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:

(I) Does the Department of Social Security take into
account fluctuations in the exchange rates between the Australian dollar and the English pound in the process of deter-
mining to what extent the receipt of British pension affects the amount of Australian pension paid to a person receiving both an Australian and a British pension.

(2) [s it a fact that during the first 6 months of 1981, the exchange rate between the Australian dollar and the British
pound has fallen from approximately $A2 for  Istg to $A 1.80 for  Istg.

(3) What has been the effect of this fluctuation on a pensioner receiving the maximum entitlements for British and Australian pensions.

(4) Is it a fact that while the exchange value of the British pension has fallen during this period the Department of Social
Security has not altered the assessed Australian dollar value of the British pension during that period.

(5) [f so, has this resulted in pensioners being disadvan-
taged because (a) of reduced real income from their British pension and (b) no compensation is made of the reduced real
income in respect of the assessment by the Department of extra income for means test purposes.

(6) How regularly are adjustments in national exchange

rates made to overcome this situation.

.

(7) Will a new exchange rate apply from 4 June 1981.

(8) Will the re-adjustment be made retrospective to take account of fluctuations during the six month period referred
to in part (2); if not, why not.

Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has
provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) Yes.

(2) Yes. The Commonwealth Trading Bank on-demand air mail buying rate for pounds sterling varied from $A2.0044 on 2 January 1981 to $A1.796 on 29 May 1981.

(3) to (7) It is impracticable to ensure that Australian pensions, when affected by British pensions, are assessed on
exchange rates obtained by pensioners from banks on a dayto-day basis. Accordingly, for the purpose of calculating the
amount of Australian pension to be paid to a British pensioner, a notional exchange rate is set, based on the Commonwealth Trading Bank on-demand air mail buying rate (the
CTB rate). The general rule has been to alter the notional exchange rate when it differed by five cents or more Aus-
tralian per one pound sterling from the CTB rate if that appeared to be stable. Consequently, to the extent that the
notional exchange rate differs from the CTB rate, people entitled to both British and Australian pensions may receive less or more than their actual entitlem'ent at any given time. Pri-
marily because of the continuous and substantial decline in the value of sterling and the necessary lead-time to effect
changes, it has not been possible at all times to keep the notional exchange rate in line with the CTB rate.

Since I January 1981, the Department of Social Security has altered the notional exchange rate as follows:

from $A2.05 to $A2.00 =  I stg with effect from pension

payday 12/2/81

.

from $A2.00 to $A 1.80 =  I stg with effect from pension

payday 4/6/81

'

from $A 1.80 to $A I. 70 =  I stg with effect from pension payday 16/7/81
from $A1.70 to $AI.60=  I stg with effect from pension payday 27/8/81
Under normal circumstances, the notional exchange rate would have been altered to relate to published exchange rates for the first week in April 1981 but because of machine Lime limitations consequent on the need to pay indexation increases in May 1981, it was not possible to adjust to a new exchange rate until the payday 4 June 1981. The notional exchange rate to apply from that time was fixed at  I stg = $A1.80 having regard to the published exchange rates applying early in May 1981. Subsequent adjustments are detailed above.
The procedure for fixing notional exchange rates is an adminstrative measure designed to avoid the many thousands of transactions that otherwise would be required if adjustments were made for minor variations in the exchange rates. It is also designed to enable minor variations in published exchange rates to be compensated, over time, by balancing variations in the other direction, so providing pensioners with equitable treatment overall. Where the direction of variation is consistent over an extended period, adjustments in the notional exchange rate lag behind the true position but this is the case whether the published exchange rate favours or disadvantages the pensioner.
(8) It has been decided to make retrospective adjustments in Australian pensions to compensate people for losses they have sustained in recent months. Because of the very large number of cases involved it will not be possible to ensure that adjusting payments are made immediately in all instances, but arrangements are being made to process such payments as quickly as possible.
Medical Research: Expenditure
(Question No. 2406)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Does the paper entilled 'Strategies for funding in the 1980's' prepared by the Public Affairs Commillee of the Australian Society for Medical Research indicate that Australia, through the National Health and Medical Research Council, expended only 85 cents per head of population on medical research in 1978.
(2) Does the paper also show that Canada, New Zealand, the United Kingdom and the United States of America spent on medical research $1.86, $1.29, $1.99 and $10.77 per head respectively.
(3) Does the paper estimate that only 0.43 per cent of the 1980 direct Commonwealth health bill was spent on the Council's budget.
(4) Does Australia expe,nd a sum .sufficient for effective medical research, or is the sum,expended representative of the Government's priority for medical research, in view of overall budgetary restraints in the Pfevailing economic conditions.
(5) What constitutes medical research so far as the Council is concerned.
(6) Is it a fact that, despite a real increase in the total 1981 allocation for research, the perceniage of new projects funded (346 out of 770) was lower than for (a) 1977, (b) 1978 and (c) 1979.
(7) [s there a need to extend the period for which research projects are funded beyond one year; if so, will he consider implementing this proposal.
(8) Is it a fact that, of those applicants for grants in 1980, approximately 100 were unsuccessful.

1648 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Answers (0 Qu(!stions

'(9) Of those sllccessful applicants, have approximately 200 been funded for one year only,
(10) Does the re-application of these projects for consideration for funding constitute an excessive workload for both the applicants and the Council, as the assessors.
(II) Does the position paper describe this situation as one of crisis proportions.
(12) What proposals has he received and will he consider taking steps to resolve this situation.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Yes. The paper does not, however, acknowledge the doubtful validity of such comparisons because of the different bases on which national governments provide support for medical research.
(3) Yes. This figure however is obtained from a comparison of support for medical research through the National Health and Medical Research Council (NH & MRC) with overall Commonwealth expenditure on health. As such it ignores a major contribution to medical research provided by the Government through the Tertiary Education Commission and the Department of Health. The figure also fails to acknowledge the substantial contribution to Australian medical research from local private funding bodies.
(4) Although the Australian medical research community is relatively small, it has achieved international recognition for its contributions to medical research which bears witness to the effectiveness of the research efforts. The Government accepts this, and has continued to pursue its policy of increasing support for medical research as and when economic conditions allow.
(5) Medical research is defined as including all research in which human subjects are involved and all investigations in physiology, biochemistry, pathology and other medical sciences and disciplines reasonably likely to have a close bearing on problems of human health.
(6) Yes. However, the percentage of new projects funded in 1981 was 44.9 per cent, which is considerably higher than the 1980 figure of 36.3 per cent, despite a major increase in the number of applications in 1981.
(7) There is no restriction of project grants to one year funding. The present scheme allows support for one, two or three years.
(8) No. As part (6) of the question states, 770 applications were received in 1980 for support in 1981, of which 346 were supported.
(9) Yes.
(10) to (12) It is acknowledged that. support of medical research projects for one year only is, in many cases, less efficient scientifically and administratively than longer term support. The NH & MRC is investigating methods of reducing the number of one year grants and any recommendations will be carefully considered.
Overservicing by Doctors
(Question No. 2424)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(1) Has his Department any record of the numbers of cases and costs of overservicing by doctors.
(2) What resources does his Department devote to recouping sums associated with overservicing.
: (3) How many cases have been reviewed in the period January to 2 June 1981'

(4) In what States have Medical Services Committees of In-

quiry been established.

.

(5) Do these Committees use uniform criteria in each State.

(6) What sums in assessing overservicing have' been recovered due to the actions of the MediCal Service
Committees.

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) My Department has been concerned to determine the extent of overservicing and to develop appropriate measures to bring under control any unnecessary medical benefits
expenditure.

A large-scale (computer-based) fraud and overservicing detection system has been developed and I have asked my De-
partment to undertake full analysis and to produce a plan of
action for consideration by the Government.

Overservicing is defined as services provided by doctorS
that, in the terms of the Health Insurance Act, are not reasonably necessary for the adequate medical care of the patients
concerned. I must make clear that the Health Insurance Act deals with eligibility for medical benefits and not situations where patients and their doctors make arrangements for the
provision of medical services outside the MedicalBenefits
Scheme, for such things as cosmetic surgery. It is the Government's decision that priority for medical benefits expenditure
is to be given to what are regarded as the more essential medical services and that for other medical services patients are free to make other arrangements with their doctors.

(2) It is difficult to be specific as to the resources my De-

partment devotes .to recoup sums associated with overservic-

ing as many resources are used in a multitude of tasks, such as

the computing resources used concurrently in the payment of

Commonwealth medical benefits, the production of statistics

and the production of profiles for the fraud and overservicing

detection system. However, seventeen officers are directly

associated with the support work for medical counsellors and

the eight State Medical or Optometrical Services Committees

of Inquiry and two Review Tribunals. As well, a large part of

the time of the seven medical counsellors currently employed

by my Department is directed to counselling doctors regard-

ing their patterns of practice, and to giving of advice, or of

warnings where appropriate.

.

(3) The number of cases reviewed in the period I January 1981 t02June 1981 involved:

(a) 195 doctors, who were counselled by Departmental medical officers; and

(b) 19 cases of possible overservicing which are undepconsideration by Medical Services Committees of Inquiry.

(4) Medical Services Committees of Inquiry have been es-

tablished in each State (New South Wales includes the Aus-

tralian Capital Territory, and South Australia the Northern

Territory for this purpose). The Health Insurance Act allows

for the establishment of more than one CQmmittee in' each

State and a second Committee has been established in New

South Wales.

.

(5) These Committees have regard to the same definition of
overservicing in the Health Insurance Act and are provided with general guidelines as to operating procedures. However,
each Committee is independent and uses its own professional
experience and judgement in determining whether excessive medical services have been provided.

(6) Effective determinations made since the cOf!1mence-

ment of the operation of the Medical Services Committees of

Inquiry system in 1977 involve the recovery of $) 15,419.37

Commonwealth or fund benefits.

.

Pensions: Persons Residing Overseas
(Question No. 2522)
Mr Charles Jones asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:

Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) (a) Statistics of Social Security pensions paid to persons residing permanently overseas by country of residence are not available. (b) Statistics of Social Security pensions paid overseas to persons residing overseas, who stated at the time of departure that they were leaving Australia permanently, are available by
type of pension and are sho,wn in Table 2 below. or the 17,230

Immigration: Timorese Citizens
(Question No. 2726)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:

(1) Further to his answer to question No. 986 (Hansard,5 May 1981, page 2008), are the 343 persons referred to in part (I) as being on the list of persons permitted to migrate to Australia from East Timor, ethnic Timorese.
(2) Does the special humanitarian program referred to in part (3) of his reply provide for the entry of relatives of

\

Answers to Questions

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1651

AustraliaJTimorese residents who are currently resident within East Timor.
(3) Does the answer to part (3) of question No. 986 mean that only 200 ethnic Timorese, out of the 2,668 persons originally nominated, have been permitted to migrate to Australia.
(4) Will he indicate the exact nature of the difficulties in the way of sending a special migration mission to Dili to process applications from the remaining persons on the agreed list.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) As migrant statistics do not reRect ethnic origin, it is not possible to provide the information requested.
(2) The main objective in establishing the Special Humanitarian Program for Timorese last year was to assist those whose lives had been disrupted by events of 1975, and who had been living temporarily outside East Timor (particularly in Portugal) and experiencing settlement difficulties. The program provides for the sponsorship of any Timorese relative of Australain residents who was outside Timor before the end of September. Relatives of Australian residents still in East Timor are eligible for sponsorship under family reunion policy.
(3) No. Information about those of the 2,668 persons who did not qualify for inclusion in the 'agreed list' and who may have subsequently emigrated is not available. Some, however, may have already come here under the Special Humanitarian Program. This is in addition to those who have arrived under both the relaxed and normal family reunion programs. To date, 1,200 persons have been visaed for entry under the Special Humanitarian Program.
(4) As I indicated in ,my reply to question 986, the operation of a special migration team in Dili would depend upon the co-operation of the Indonesian authorities. It is clear from recent discussions with these authorities that it would not be practicable to propose sending another mission to Dili.
Brisbane Airport Development
(Question No. 2756)
Mrs Darling' asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:

(I) Has his attention been drawn to a resolution of the Queensland Liberal Party's Convention recommending to the Federal Government that the proposed opening date for extensions to Brisbane Airport be brought forward.
(2) If so, what action has he taken with respect to this suggestion.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes, the Government has noted details of the resolutions carried at the 1979 Queensland Liberal Party Convention.
(2) As part of the Commonwealth's commitment to the redevelopment of Brisbane Airport, it was announced in the Budget that an estimated $29.9m will be spent on site preparation work in 1981-82. It was also announced that the Commonwealth had approved expenditure of $2m on extensions to the international terminal building to cater for increasing passenger loads. These extensions are scheduled for completion prior to the commencement of the Commonwealth Games in September 1982.

Brisbane Airport
(Question No. 2775)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:

Answers to Questions

Immigration: Timorese Citizens
(Question No. 2786)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) When was the list of Timorese citizens permitted to migrate from East Timor to Australia agreed to byboth the Indonesian and Australian Governments.
(2) How many persons were included on the original list.
(3) How many of those who have been permitted to leave East Timor and who have arrived in Australia have been of (a) Chinese, (b) native East Timoreseand (c) other origins.
(4) What difficulties have been experienced by those on the original list in obtaining permission from the Indonesian Government to migrate.
(5) How many of the 650 relatives of Australian residents referred to by him in his reply to question No. 986 (Hansard, 5 May 1981, page 2007) have come from (a) Portugal, (b) East Timor and (c) other countries.
(6) What are the difficulties in sending a special migration mission to Dili and are these difficulties outweighted by the humanitarian considerations of the proposal.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) July i978.
(2) On the assumption that the honourable member was not referring to the list which was negotiated between the Australian and Indonesian Governments in July 1978, the original list included 2,668 persons. As a result of the negotiations in July 1978 this list was reduced to 625 persons.
(3) As statistics of migrants do not identify ethnic origin, it is not possible to provide the information sought.
(4) The difficulties claimed by some East Timorese in obtaining permission to migrate under the 'agreed list' program have been the subject of continuing discussions with the Indonesian authorities. We have recently received positive assurances from the Indonesian Government that family reunion movements will not be impeded.
(5) An estimated 80 per cent of the evacuees arrived from Portugal. The remainder came from other sources such as Hong Kong and Taiwan, excluding (of course) East Timor.
(6) As I indicated in my reply to question 986, the operation of a special migration mission in Dili would depend upon the co-operation of the Indonesian authorities. It is clear from recent discussions with these authorities that it would not be practicable to propose sending another mission to Dili.
Cathay Pacific Airways: Services from Brisbane to Hong Kong ,
(Question No. 2824)
Mr Jull asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
(I) Has Cathay Pacific Airways applied for rights to operate services from Brisbane to Hong Kong and has this application been rejected; if so, why.
(2) Which other international airlines have applied for rights into Brisbane since 1 January 1981 and which have been accepted.
Mr Hunt-The answer td the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) In May 1981 the United Kingdom sought Al\stralia's agreement in principle to services by a Bri.tish airline between Hong Kong and Brisbane. The present level of demand was not considered sufficient to justify an increase in capacity between East Australia and Hong Kong.
Passengers can travel between Brisbane and Hong Kong without any cost penalty, compared to passengers travelling to/from Sydney and Melbourne, because fares are set at the same levels for all three East Australian cities.
The United Kingdom indicated it would raise the question again, when it had a more precise idea of the time at which a British airline would wish to commence Brisbane-Hong Kong services.
(2) Philippine Airlines was granted traffic rights at Brisbane in March 1981. PAL currently operate one service per week between Brisbane and Manila. No other applications were received.
Alleged Design Flaw in Ford Cortinas
(Question No. 2829)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 25 August 1981:
(I) Is he able to say whether the Australian Automobile Association alleged that a design flaw exists in the brass connections linking fuel lines with carburettors in 2 litre Ford Cortinas constructed between 1972 and 1974.
(2) Have investigations into this matter been conducted at the request of the Government; if so, what are the details.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Under the terms of an industry code of practice endorsed by the Australian Transport Advisory Council in 1972 it is the manufacturer's responsibility to rectify safety related vehicle defects when he is satisfied that they stem from a design deficiency. I understand that there are on-going discussions in this particular case between the Australian Automobile Association and the manufacturer as to the source of the problem reported. My colleague, the Minister for Transport, informs me that the Australian Transport Advisory Council has recently established a task group to review the operation of the industry code and the evidence and the negotiations between the parties in this case will most likely have some relevance to the arrangements which that Council will adopt for the future.

(Question No. 2839)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Prime Minister, and all other Ministers, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:

(2) If the scrutiny referred to in part (I) has not been completed, when will it'be finalised and the results reported to Parliament.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1653

(I) and (2) Ministers are at present examining all statutory authorities within their portfolios with a view to recommending those that should be terminated, continued or reviewed in depth. The investigations are to be completed so that the results can be forwarded to the Prime Minister within the next three months. The Government will then be in a position to give further consideration to matters raised in those reports. The,outcome of the deliberations will be made public in due course.
Statutory Authorities under the Minister for Social Security
(Question No. 2851)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister representing the Minister for: Social Security, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:
(I) Further to the Review of Commonwealth Functions which called for Ministers to scrutinise all authorities within their portfolios (Hansard, 30 April 1981, page 1850) which statutory authorities within the Minister's portfolio are to be (a) terminated, (b) continued and (c) reviewed in depth.
(2) If the scrutiny referred to in part (I) has not been completed, when will it be finalised and the results reported to Parliament.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) and (2) Before I July 1981, the only statutory authorities within my portfolio were the Compensation Tribunal and the Commissioner for Employees' Compensation.
Following the Review of Commonwealth Functions, it '>ras decided that the Compensation Tribunal be transferred to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal from I July 1981 with support services being provided by the Attorney-General's Department, and that the Office of the Commissioner for Employees' Compensation be continued.

Consultants: Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet
(Question No. 2874)
Mr Morrison asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:
(I) Does page 2 of the 1980-81 Annual Report of his Department list O. Harries and A. Wilson as consultants; if so, when did each become consultants and what are the terms and conditions of their employment.
(2) In what activities are they engaged as consultants.
(3) Have either been associated with the Commonwealth Public Service previously; if so, in what capacity and for what periods.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (3) Professor O. Harries commenced a full time engagement with the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet on 27 March 1981 with remuneration at the level 4, Second Division salary level. He provides advice on a variety of policy matters including Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting policy issues and related subjects. Prior to his engagement he had been employed with the Department of Foreign Affairs.
Dr A. Wilson is an officer of the Australian Atomic Energy Commission. Since January 1981 his services have been available as a part time adviser on energy matters. Dr Wilson continues to be paid by the Australian Atomic Energy Commission and receives no additional salary in respect of his part time advisory role.
Transcontinental Railway: Use of Radioactive Rocks as Ballast
(Question No. 2875)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:
(I) Was radioactive ballast, taken from uranium mines in South Australia, used on the up-graded Transcontinental railway; if so, what quantity;
(2) What types of radioactive rocks were used;
(3) When were these rocks laid as ballast;
(4) Who were the contractors who laid the ballast;
(5) How much was paid for it;
(6) Between what locations on the line was the radioactive ballast used;
(7) What is the current level of radioactivity in the relevant area;
(8) How often is the level of radioactivity monitored;

1654 REPRESENTATIVES 22 September 1981

Answers to Questions

(9) When was it last monitored.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes, 254,000 cubic yards of tailings were used from the Radium Hill Mine heavy media section of the concentration plant.
(2) The types of rocks used were non-mineralised rock (country rock) plus small quantities of Davidite (IronTitanium Mineral with small amounts of Uranium).
(3) The rocks were laid as ballast between 1964 and 1967.
(4) The ballast was laid by South Australian Railways Day Labour Force.
(5) The cost and delivery of the ballast totalled $296,1 14.
(6) The ballast was used between Winnininnie and Cockburn.
(7) Tests conductd by the Health Commission of South Australia show that results are typical of a background for a granite area which does not constitute a health hazard.
(8) The level of radioactivity over this section is not monitored.
(9) The tests conducted by the Health Commission were carried out early in 1981.
Canberra Army Reserve Unit
(Question No. 2897)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
(I) Is it a fact that the Canberra Army Reserve units have cancelled Monday night parades; if so, does this constitute a reduction in the amount of training time available.
(2) If the training time has been reduced, what is the reason.
(3) Have similar cancellations taken place elsewhere.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) to (3) No. Individual unit training programmes are at the discretion of unit Commanding Officers. The Canberra Army Reserve Unit (3 RNSWR) has structured its night parades principally for Officer and Senior NCO training. Training of soldiers will be conducted on selected night parades, weekends and annual field exercises. There has been no reduction in the total training time available.

Water Resources: National Objectives
(Question No. 3006)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and Energy, upon notice, on 9 September 1981 :
(I) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the ASTEC Report entitled 'Basic Research and National Objectives' which advanced National Objectives for Water Resources as being to (a) develop and apply improved mamigement practices, integrated with land use planning and management, which reverse trends in the deterioration of water quality and associated land resources, (b) devise and l\pply improved practices to minimise water use and eliminate waste, and develop alternative water resources such as desalination, re-use, and the maximum use of water from high-rainfall areas and (c) improve understanding of subterranean water resources, their sustained yield management and utilisation.
(2) If so, is it a fact that the report notes that each objective is of national as distinct from regional or local importance.

Answers to Questions

22 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1655

(3) Does the Minister support these 3 objectives; if so, (a)
what action will the Government take to implement this basic research and (b) through which agency will it do so.

Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) Yes.

(2) Yes.

(3) Yes. These objectives are consistent with those iden-
tified in the Government's water policy statement (House of Representatives Hansard. pages 1211-1213,28 March 1979).

(a) Most of the projects funded by the Government under the research program of the Australian Water Resources
Council are directly relevant to these objectives. In addition,
the Commonwealth-States water resources assessment program has considerably improved knowledge of the quantity and quality of Australia's groundwater resources.

(b) As far as practicable, research related to these objec-

tives will be considered within the scope of the research

program of the Australian Water Resources Council. It is also

a matter for consideration by CSIRO, which undertakes the

largest water research program in Australia.
